\section{PREFACE}

This series of physics problems and solutions, which consists of seven parts - Mechanics, Electromagnetism, Optics, Atomic, Nuclear and Particle Physics, Thermodynamics and Statistical Physics, Quantum Mechanics, Solid State Physics - contains a selection of 2550 problems from the graduate school entrance and qualifying examination papers of seven U.S. universities - California University Berkeley Campus, Columbia University, Chicago University, Massachusetts Institute of Technology, New York State University Buffalo Campus, Princeton University, Wisconsin University as well as the CUSPEA and C. C. Ting's papers for selection of Chinese students for further studies in U.S.A. and their solutions which represent the effort of more than 70 Chinese physicists.

The series is remarkable for its comprehensive coverage. In each area the problems span a wide spectrum of topics while many problems overlap several areas. The problems themselves are remarkable for their versatility in applying the physical laws and principles, their uptodate realistic situations, and their acanty demand on mathematical skills. Many of the problems involve order of magnitude calculations which one often requires in an experimental situation for estimating a quantity from a simple model. In short, the exercises blend together the objectives of enhancement of one's underat anding of the physical principles and practical applicability.

The solutions as presented generally just provide a guidance to solving the probelms rather than step by step manipulation and leave much to the student to work out for him/herself, of whom much is demanded of the basic knowledge in physics. Thus the series would provide an invaluable complement to the textbooks.

In editing no attempt has been made to unify the physical terms and symbols. Rather, they are left to the setters' and solvers' own preference so as to reflect the realistic situation of the usage today. The present volume for Optics consists of three parts: Geometrical Optics, Physical Optics, Quantum Optics, and comprises 160 problems.

\author{
Lim Yung-kuo \\ Editor
}



\section{INTRODUCTION}

Solving problems in school work is the exercise of mental faculties, and examination problems are usually the pick of the problems in school work. Working out problems is a necessary and important aspect of the learning of Physics.

The American University Ph. D. Qualifying Questions and Solutions is a series of seven volumes. The subjects of the volumes and their respective referees (in parentheses) are as follows:

1. Mechanics (Qiang Yuan-qi, Gu En-pu, Cheng Jia-fu, Li Ze-hua, Yang De-tian)

2. Electromagnetism (Zhao Shu-ping, You Jun-han, Zhu Jun-jie)

3. Optics (Bai Gui-ru, Guo Guang-can)

4. Atomic, Nuclear and Particle Physics (Jin Huai-cheng, Yang Baoshong, Fan Yang-mei)

5. Thermodynamics and Statistical Physics (Zheng Jiu-ren)

6. Quantum Mechanics (Zhang Yong-de, Zhu Dong-pei, Fan Hong-yi)

7. Solid State Physics and Comprehensive Topics (Zhang Jia-l≈©, Zhou You-yuan, Zhang Shi-ling)

These books cover almost all aspects of university physics and contain 2550 problems, most of which are solved in detail.

These problems have been carefully chosen from a collection of 3100 problems some of which came from the China-U.S.A. Physics Examination and Application Programme and Ph.D. Qualifying Examination on Experimental High Energy Physics sponsored by Chao Chong Ting, while the other from the graduate preliminary or qualifying examination questions of the following seven top American universities during the last decade: Columbia University, University of California at Berkeley, Massachusetts Institute of Technology, University of Wisconsin, University of Chicago, Princeton University, State University of New York at Buffalo.

In general, examination problems on physics in American universities do not involve too much mathematics. Rather, they can be categorized into the following three types. Many of the problems that involve the various frontier subjects and overlapping domains of science have been selected by the professors directly from their own research and show a "modern style". Some of the problems involve a wide field and require a quick mind to analyse, while the others are often simple to solve but are practical and require a full "touch of physics." We think it reasonable to take these problems as a reflection, to some extent, of the characteristics of American science and culture, as well as the tenet of American education.

This being so, we believe it worthwhile to collect and solve these problems and then introduce them to the students and teachers, even though the effort involved is formidable. Nearly a hundred teachers and graduate students took part in this time-consuming task.

There are 160 problems in this volume, which is divided into three parts: part I consists of 41 problems in geometric optics, part II consists of 89 problems in wave optics, part III consists of 30 problems in quantum optics.

The depth of knowledge involved in solving these problems is not beyond the contents of common textbooks on optics used in colleges and universities in China, although the scope of the knowledge and techniques needed in solving some of the problems go beyond what we are usually familiar with. Furthermore, some new scientific research results (e.g. some newly developed lasers) are introduced in the problems. This will not only enhance the understanding of the established theories and knowledge, but also encourage the interaction between teaching and research which cannot but enliven academic thoughts and excite the mind.

The physicists who contributed to solving the problems in this volume are Shi De-xiu, Yao Kun, Lu Hong-jun, Chen Xiang-li, Gu Chun, Han Wenhai and Wu Zhi-qiang. The initial translation from Chinese into English was carried out by Xuan Zhi-hua. Some revisions have been made in this English edition bv the combilers. the translator and the editor.

\section{PART 1 GEOMETRICAL OPTICS}

A rainbow is produced by:
(a) refraction of sunlight by water droplets in the atmosphere.
(b) reflection of sunlight by clouds.
(c) refraction of sunlight in the human eye.

\title{
Solution:
}

\author{
The answer is (a).
}

\section{2}

A horizontal ray of light passes through a prism of index $1.50$ and apex angle $4^{\circ}$ and then strikes a vertical mirror, as shown in the figure. Through what angle must the mirror be rotated if after reflection the ray is to be horisontal?

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-015.jpg?height=504&width=584&top_left_y=956&top_left_x=377)

Fig. $1.1$

\section{Solution:}

As the apex angle is very small $\left(\alpha=4^{\circ}\right)$, the angle of deviation $\delta$ can be obtained approximately:

$$
\delta=(n-1) \alpha=(1.5-1) \times 4^{\circ}=2^{\circ} .
$$

From Fig. $1.2$ we see that if the reflected ray is to be horizontal, the mirror must be rotated clockwise through an angle $\gamma$ given by

$$
\gamma=\frac{\delta}{2}=1^{\circ} \text {. }
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-016.jpg?height=314&width=651&top_left_y=242&top_left_x=439)

Fig. $1.2$

\section{8}

A narrow beam of light is incident on a $30^{\circ}-60^{\circ}-90^{\circ}$ prism as shown (Fig. 1.3). The index of refraction of the prism is $n=2.1$. Show that the entire beam emerges either from the right-hand face, or back along the incident path.

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-016.jpg?height=303&width=319&top_left_y=1043&top_left_x=260)

Fig. $1.3$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-016.jpg?height=223&width=415&top_left_y=1093&top_left_x=842)

Fig. $1.4$

\section{Solution:}

As seen from Fig. 1.4, for normal incidence at the bottom face the angle of incidence at $B$ is $30^{\circ}$, and that at $C$ is $60^{\circ}$, both of which are larger than the critical angle of the prism,

$$
\theta_{c}=\sin ^{-1} \frac{1}{n}=28^{\circ} 26^{\prime} .
$$

Hence the ray is totally reflected at $B$ and C. Also, the ray is partially reflected back at the bottom and the right-hand faces. Therefore, the entire beam emerges either from the right-hand face, or back along the incident path. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-017.jpg?height=289&width=202&top_left_y=202&top_left_x=609)

Fig. $1.5$

A glass cube has a refractive index of 1.5. A light beam enters the top face obliquely and then strikes the side of the cube. Does light emerge from this side? Explain your answer.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

Assume that the angles of incidence and refraction at the top face are $i_{1}$ and $i_{1}^{\prime}$ respectively. According to Snell's law of refraction,

$$
\sin i_{1}=n \sin i_{1}^{\prime} \text {. }
$$

From the geometry (see Fig. 1.5) $i_{1}^{\prime}+i_{2}=90^{\circ}$, where $i_{2}$ is the angle of incidence at the right side. Thus, we have

$$
\sin i_{1}=n \cos i_{2},
$$

or

$$
i_{2}=\cos ^{-1}\left(\frac{\sin i_{1}}{n}\right) \text {. }
$$

When $i_{1}=90^{\circ}, i_{2}$ has the minimum value

$$
i_{2}=\cos ^{-1} \frac{1}{1.5}=48^{\circ} 10^{\prime}>i_{c}=42^{\circ} \text { (critical angle). }
$$

\section{Hence no light emerges from this side.}

\section{5}

A glass rod of rectangular cross-section is bent into the shape shown in the Fig. 1.6. A parallel beam of light falls perpendicularly on the flat surface $A$. Determine the minimum value of the ratio $R / d$ for which all light entering the glass through surface $A$ will emerge from the glass through surface B. The index of refraction of the glass is $1.5$.

(Wisconsin) 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-018.jpg?height=286&width=271&top_left_y=342&top_left_x=391)

Fig. $1.6$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-018.jpg?height=419&width=402&top_left_y=209&top_left_x=757)

Fig. $1.7$

\section{Solution:}

Consider the representative rays shown in Fig. 1.7. A ray entering the glass through surface $A$ and passing along the inner side of the rod will be reflected by the outer side with the smallest angle $\alpha$, at which the reflected ray is tangent to the inner side. We have to consider the conditions under which the ray will undergo total internal reflection before reaching $B$.

If $\alpha>\theta_{c}$, the critical angle, at which total internal reflection occurs, all the incident beam will emerge through the surface B. Hence we require

$$
\sin \alpha>\frac{1}{n} .
$$

The geometry gives

$$
\sin \alpha=\frac{R}{(R+d)} .
$$

Therefore

$$
\frac{R}{R+d} \geq \frac{1}{n},
$$

or

$$
\left(\frac{R}{d}\right)_{\min }=\frac{1}{n-1}=\frac{1}{1.5-1}=2 .
$$

\section{6}

A small fish, four feet below the surface of Lake Mendota is viewed through a simple thin converging lens with focal length 30 feet. If the lens is 2 feet above the water surface (Fig. 1.8), where is the image of the fish seen by the observer? Assume the fish lies on the optical axis of the lens and that $n_{\text {air }}=1.0, n_{\text {water }}=1.33$.

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-019.jpg?height=194&width=412&top_left_y=439&top_left_x=262)

Fig. $1.8$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-019.jpg?height=371&width=252&top_left_y=348&top_left_x=900)

Fig. $1.8$

\section{Solution:}

An object at $P$ in water appears to be at $P^{\prime}$ as seen by an observer in air, as Fig. $1.9$ shows. The paraxial light emitted by $P$ is refracted at the water surface, for which

$$
1.33 \sin i_{1}=\sin i_{2} .
$$

As $i_{1}, i_{2}$ are very small, we have the approximation $1.33 i_{1}=i_{2}$. Also,

$$
i_{2}=\alpha \approx \frac{\overline{\mathrm{OQ}}}{\overline{\mathrm{OP}^{\prime}}}, \quad i_{1}=\beta \approx \frac{\overline{\mathrm{OQ}}}{\overline{\mathrm{QP}}} .
$$

Hence, we have

$$
\overline{\mathrm{OP}^{\prime}}=\frac{1}{1.33} \cdot \overline{\mathrm{OP}}=3 \mathrm{ft} .
$$

Let the distance between the apparent location of the fish and the center of the lens be $u$, then

$$
u=2+\overline{O P^{\prime}}=5 \mathrm{ft} .
$$

From $\frac{1}{f}=\frac{1}{u}+\frac{1}{v}$, we have

$$
v=-6 \mathrm{ft} \text {. }
$$

Therefore, the image of the fish is still where the fish is, four feet below the 

\section{7}

The index of refraction of glass can be increased by diffusing in impurities. It is then possible to make a lens of constant thickness. Given a disk of radius $a$ and thickness $d$, find the radial variation of the index of refraction $n(r)$ which will produce a lens with focal length $F$. You may assume a thin lens $(d \ll a)$.

(Chicago)

\section{Solution:}

Let the refractive index of the material of the disk be $n$ and the radial distribution of the refractive index of the impurity-diffused disk be represented by $n(r)$, with $n(0)=n_{0}$. Incident plane waves entering the lens refract and converge at the focus $F$ as shown in Fig. 1.10. We have

$$
\left|n(r)-n_{0}\right| d=-\sqrt{F^{2}+r^{2}}+F,
$$

i.e.,

$$
n(r)=n_{0}-\frac{\sqrt{F^{2}+r^{2}}-F}{d} .
$$

For $F>r$, we obtain

$$
n(r)=n_{0}-\frac{r^{2}}{2 d F} .
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-020.jpg?height=292&width=367&top_left_y=1107&top_left_x=591)

Fig. $1.10$

\section{8}

The index of refraction of air at $300 \mathrm{~K}$ and 1 atmosphere pressure is $1.0003$ in the middle of the visible spectrum. Assuming an isothermal atmosphere at $300 \mathrm{~K}$, calculate by what factor the earth's atmosphere would have to be more dense to cause light to bend around the earth with the earth's curvature at sea level. (In cloudless skies we could then watch sunset all night, in principle, but with an image of the sun drastically compressed vertically.) You may assume that the index of refraction $n$ has the property that $n-1$ is proportional to the density. (Hint: Think of Fermat's Principle.) The 1/e height of this isothermal atmosphere is 8700 metres.

(UC, Berkeley)

\section{Solution:}

We are given that

$$
n(r)-1=\rho e^{-\frac{r-f}{s} r b},
$$

where $R=6400 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~m}$ is the earth's radius and $\rho$ is the density coefficient of air. Then

$$
\begin{gathered}
n(r)=1+\rho e^{-\frac{5 \pi}{500}}, \\
\frac{d n(r)}{d r}=n^{\prime}(r)=-\frac{1}{8700} \rho e^{-\frac{5-8}{500}} .
\end{gathered}
$$

It is also given that air is so dense that it makes light bend around the earth with the earth's curvature at sea level, as shown in Fig. 1.11.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-021.jpg?height=264&width=270&top_left_y=879&top_left_x=571)

Fig. $1.11$

The optical path length from $A$ to $B$ is

$$
l=n(r) r \theta .
$$

\section{According to Fermat's Principle, the optical path length from A to B should be an extremum. Therefore,}

$$
\frac{d l}{d r}=\left[n^{\prime}(r) r+n(r)\right] \theta=0,
$$

i.e.,

$$
n^{\prime}(r)=\frac{-n(r)}{r} .
$$

\section{Substituting (3) into (2) yields}

$$
\frac{1}{8700} \rho e^{-\frac{r-\pi}{800}}=\frac{n(r)}{r} .
$$

At sea level, $r=R=6400 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~m}$. This with (1) and (4) yields

$$
\frac{\rho \times 6400 \times 10^{3}}{8700}=1+\rho
$$

giving

$$
\rho=0.00136 .
$$

At sea level, i.e., at $300 \mathrm{~K}$ and 1 atmosphere pressure, $n_{0}-1=\rho_{0}=0.0003$. Therefore

$$
\frac{\rho}{\rho_{0}}=4.53 .
$$

Thus only if the air were $4.53$ times as dense as the real air would light bend around the earth with the earth's curvature at sea level.

1009

Incident parallel rays make an angle of $5^{\circ}$ with the axis of a diverging lens $-20 \mathrm{~cm}$ in focal length. Locate the image.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

$20 \times \tan 5^{\circ}=1.75 \mathrm{~cm}$. The image is a virtual point image in the focal plane $1.75 \mathrm{~cm}$ off the optical axis.

\section{0}

A thin lens with index of refraction $n$ and radii of curvature $R_{1}$ and $R_{2}$ is located between 2 media with indices of refraction $n_{1}$ and $n_{2}$ as shown (Fig. 1.12). If $S_{1}$ and $S_{2}$ are the object and image distances respectively, and $f_{1}$ and $f_{2}$ the respective focal lengths, show that

$$
\frac{f_{1}}{S_{1}}+\frac{f_{2}}{S_{2}}=1 \text {. }
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-023.jpg?height=320&width=387&top_left_y=215&top_left_x=157)

Fig. $1.12$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-023.jpg?height=300&width=634&top_left_y=188&top_left_x=604)

Fig. $1.13$

\section{Solution:}

First we study the relation between $f_{1}, f_{2}$ and $R_{1}, R_{2}, n_{1}, n_{2}, n$. As Fig. $1.13$ shows, a ray parallel to the axis is refracted at $Q$ and crosses the axis at the second focal point $F_{2} ;$ a ray along the axis passes through $F_{2}$ also. As the optical lengths of the two rays are equal, we have

$$
n_{1} \overline{\mathrm{P}_{1}^{\prime} \bar{Q}}+n_{2} \overline{\mathrm{QF}_{2}}=n \overline{\mathrm{P}_{1} \mathrm{P}_{2}}+n_{2} \overline{\mathrm{P}_{2} \mathrm{~F}_{2}} \text {. }
$$

As $\overline{\mathrm{QO}_{2}}=\overline{\mathrm{P}_{1} \mathrm{O}_{2}}=R_{1}$, we have

$$
\overline{\mathrm{P}_{1} \mathrm{O}}=R_{1}-\overline{\mathrm{OO}_{2}}=R_{1}-\sqrt{R_{1}^{2}-h^{2}} \approx \frac{h^{2}}{2 R_{1}},\left(\frac{h}{R_{1}}<1\right) \text {. }
$$

In the same way, we have

$$
\overline{\mathrm{P}_{2} \mathrm{O}} \approx \frac{h^{2}}{2 R_{2}} .
$$

Hence $\overline{\mathrm{P}_{1} \mathrm{P}_{2}}=\frac{h^{2}}{2}\left(\frac{1}{R_{1}}+\frac{1}{R_{2}}\right) \cdot \mathrm{As} \overline{\mathrm{P}_{1}^{\prime} \mathrm{Q}}=\overline{\mathrm{P}_{1} \mathrm{O}}$

$$
\begin{aligned}
& \overline{\overline{Q F}_{2}}=\left(h^{2}+f_{2}^{2}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}} \approx f_{2}\left(1+\frac{h^{2}}{2 f_{2}^{2}}\right) \text {, } \\
& \overline{\mathrm{P}_{2} \mathrm{~F}_{2}}=f_{2}-\overline{\mathrm{P}_{2} \mathrm{O}}=f_{2}-\frac{h^{2}}{2 R_{2}} \text {, }
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{substituting into (1) yields}

$$
\frac{n_{2}}{f_{2}}=\frac{n-n_{1}}{R_{1}}+\frac{n-n_{2}}{R_{2}} .
$$

\section{Similarly we obtain}

$$
\frac{n_{1}}{f_{1}}=\frac{n-n_{1}}{R_{1}}+\frac{n-n_{2}}{R_{2}} .
$$

Let $A$ and $A^{\prime}$ be a pair of conjugate points. We have

$$
\begin{gathered}
\overline{\mathrm{AQ}}=\left(h^{2}+S_{1}^{2}\right)^{1 / 2} \approx S_{1}\left(1+\frac{h^{2}}{2 S_{1}^{2}}\right), \overline{\mathrm{A}^{\prime} \mathrm{Q}}=S_{2}\left(1+\frac{h^{2}}{2 S_{2}^{2}}\right), \\
\overline{\mathrm{AP}_{1}}=S_{1}-\overline{\mathrm{P}_{1} \mathrm{O}}=S_{1}-\frac{h^{2}}{2 R_{1}}, \overline{\mathrm{AP}_{2}}=S_{2}-\frac{h^{2}}{2 R_{2}}
\end{gathered}
$$

Substituting these in the optical path equation

$$
n_{1} \overline{\mathrm{AQ}}+n_{2} \overline{\mathrm{A}^{\prime} \mathrm{Q}}=n_{1} \overline{\mathrm{AP}}+n \overline{\mathrm{P}_{1} \mathrm{P}_{2}}+n_{2} \overline{\mathrm{A}^{\prime} \mathrm{P}_{2}}
$$

yields

$$
\frac{n_{1}}{S_{1}}+\frac{n_{2}}{S_{2}}=\frac{n-n_{1}}{R_{1}}+\frac{n-n_{2}}{R_{2}} .
$$

Combining $(2),(3)$ and (4) yields

$$
\frac{f_{1}}{S_{1}}+\frac{f_{2}}{S_{2}}=1 .
$$

\section{1}

A line object $5 \mathrm{~mm}$ long is located $50 \mathrm{~cm}$ in front of a camera lens. The image is focussed on the film plate and is $1 \mathrm{~mm}$ long. If the film plate is moved back $1 \mathrm{~cm}$ the width of the image blurs to $1 \mathrm{~mm}$ wide. What is the $F$-number of the lens?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

Substituting $u=50 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $\frac{y}{u}=\frac{1}{5}$ in the Gaussian lens formula

$$
\frac{1}{u}+\frac{1}{v}=\frac{1}{f}
$$

gives $f=8.33 \mathrm{~cm}, v=10 \mathrm{~cm}$. From the similar triangles in Fig. $1.14$ we have

$$
\frac{D}{v}=\frac{0.1}{1} \text {, }
$$

or $D=0.1 v=1 \mathrm{~cm}$. Therefore, $F=f / D=8.33$. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-025.jpg?height=292&width=587&top_left_y=198&top_left_x=407)

Fig. $1.14$

\begin{abstract}
1012
For a camera lens, "depth of field" is how far a point object can be away from the position where it would be precisely in focus and still have the light from it fall on the film within a "circle of confusion" of some diameter, say . For a given picture derive a relation for the depth of field, $\Delta q$, as a function of the object distance $q$, the focal length of the lens, the $f$ stop and $l$. (You may consider the object distance to be much larger than the focal length.)
\end{abstract}

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-025.jpg?height=247&width=750&top_left_y=1096&top_left_x=309)

Fig. $1.15$

Solution:

The Gaussian lens formula

$$
\frac{1}{q}+\frac{1}{q^{\prime}}=\frac{1}{f}
$$

gives $\frac{d q^{\prime}}{d q}=-\left(\frac{q^{\prime}}{q}\right)^{2}$. Thus for a small deviation of the object distance (depth of field), $\Delta q$, the deviation of the image distance, is

$$
\left|\Delta q^{\prime}\right| \approx|\Delta q|\left(\frac{q^{\prime}}{q}\right)^{2} .
$$

By geometry (Fig. 1.15),

$$
\frac{\Delta q^{\prime}}{l}=\frac{\left(q^{\prime}+\Delta q^{\prime}\right)}{D} \approx \frac{q^{\prime}}{D},
$$

where $D$ is the diameter of the lens. As $q \gg f, q^{\prime} \approx f$, we obtain

$$
\Delta q \approx \frac{l q^{\prime}}{D}\left(\frac{q}{q^{\prime}}\right)^{2} \approx \frac{l f}{D}\left(\frac{q}{f}\right)^{2}=\frac{l}{F}\left(\frac{q}{f}\right)^{2},
$$

where $F=\frac{D}{f}$ is the $f$ stop of the lens.

\section{3}

Ilustrate by a sketch the position and orientation of the image of the 3 -arrow object (Fig. 1.16). The length of each arrow is $1 / 2$ unit and the point $O$ is located $3 / 2 \mathrm{~F}$ from the center of the convex lens, $(F=1$ unit). Work out the length of the image arrows.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

The image of arrow a is shown in Fig. 1.17. From the geometry we get the length of the image, which is 1 unit. By symmetry, the length of the image arrow $b$ is the same as that of itself. The arrowhead of arrow $c$ is just at the focal point $F$; therefore, its image extends from $O^{\prime}$ to infinity. Fig. $1.16$ shows the positions and orientation of these images arrows.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-026.jpg?height=276&width=600&top_left_y=1335&top_left_x=207)

Fig. $1.16$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-026.jpg?height=225&width=439&top_left_y=1336&top_left_x=901)

Fig. $1.17$

\section{4}

A 55 year old man can focus objects clearly from $100 \mathrm{~cm}$ to $300 \mathrm{~cm}$. Representing the eye as a simple lens $2 \mathrm{~cm}$ from the retina, $300 \mathrm{~cm}) ?$

(a) What is the focal length of the lens at the far point (focussed at $100 \mathrm{~cm}) ?$

(b) What is the focal length of the lens at the near point (focussed at

(c) What strength lens (focal length) must he wear in the lower part of his bifocal eyeglasses to focus at $25 \mathrm{~cm}$ ?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) $\frac{1}{f_{\text {far }}}=\frac{1}{u_{\text {far }}}+\frac{1}{v}, \quad u_{\text {far }}=300 \mathrm{~cm}, v=2 \mathrm{~cm}$.

Solving the equation yields $f_{\text {far }}=1.987 \mathrm{~cm}$.

(b) $\frac{1}{\text { fneser }^{2}}=\frac{1}{u_{\text {pear }}}+\frac{1}{v}, u_{\text {near }}=100 \mathrm{~cm}, v=2 \mathrm{~cm}$.

Solving the equation yields $f_{\text {near }}=1.961 \mathrm{~cm}$.

be

(c) In order to see an object at $25 \mathrm{~cm}$ clearly, the dioptric power should

$$
\Phi=\frac{1}{u}+\frac{1}{v}=\frac{1}{0.25}+\frac{1}{0.02}=54 \text { diopters }
$$

and the combined power is the sum of the powers of the eye and the glasses,

$$
\Phi=\Phi_{\text {eye }}+\Phi_{\text {glasses }} \cdot
$$

Thus $\Phi_{\text {slanses }}=\Phi-\Phi_{\text {eye }}=54-\frac{1}{0.01961}=3$ diopters $\left(\Phi_{\text {eye }}=\frac{1}{f \text { rear }}\right)$. He must wear $300^{\circ}$ far-sighted eyeglasses. The corresponding focal length is

$$
f_{\text {glasses }}=\frac{1}{\Phi_{\text {glasses }}}=\frac{1}{3} \mathrm{~m}=33.3 \mathrm{~cm} \text {. }
$$

\section{5}

A retro-reflector is an optical device which reflects light back directly whence it came. The most familiar retro-reflector is the reflecting corner cube, but recently the $3 \mathrm{M}$ Company invented "Scotchlite" spheres.

(a) Calculate the index of refraction $n$ and any other relevant parameters which enable a sphere to retro-reflect light.

(b) Sketch how you think Scotchlite works, and discuss qualitatively the factors which might determine the reflective efficiency of Scotchlite.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-027.jpg?height=181&width=305&top_left_y=1702&top_left_x=538)

(UC,Berkeley)

Fig. $1.18$ 

\section{Solution:}

(a) The "Scotchlite" sphere is a ball of index of refraction $n$, whose rear semi-spherical interface is a reflecting surface. The focal length in the image space, $f$, for a single refractive interface is given by

$$
f=\frac{n r}{n-1},
$$

where $r$ is the radius of the sphere. The index of refraction of air is unity. The index of refraction of the glass is chosen so that the back focal point of the front semi-spherical interface coincides with the apex of the rear semi-spherical interface (see Fig. 1.18), i.e.,

$$
f=2 r \text {. }
$$

\section{Hence $n=2$.}

(b) The rear semi-spherical interface of the "Scotchlite" sphere refects the in-coming light partially, its retro-reflectance efficiency $\eta$ being given by

$$
\eta=T^{2} R,
$$

where $T$ is the transparency of the front semi-spherical interface at which light is refracted twice, being

$$
T=\frac{4 n}{(n+1)^{2}}=0.89,
$$

and $R$ is the reflectance of the rear semi-spherical interface. Here we have assumed that no absorption occurs. For silver coating, $R=0.95$, we have

$$
\eta=0.89^{2} \times 0.95=75 \% \text {. }
$$

\section{6}

A beaded screen (Fig. 1.19) returns light back to the source if light focusses on its back surface. For use by skindivers in water $\left(n=\frac{1}{9}\right)$, of what index material should the beads be made ideally?

(Wisconsin) 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-029.jpg?height=519&width=482&top_left_y=229&top_left_x=142)

Fig. $1.19$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-029.jpg?height=472&width=482&top_left_y=234&top_left_x=769)

Fig. $1.20$

\section{Solution:}

Let the required index of refraction be $n^{\prime}$, (see Fig. 1.20). If a pencil of paraxial rays which are parallel to the axis, OP, strikes at $P$, the reflected rays will return back parallel to OP. Snell's law of refraction $n^{\prime} \sin i_{2}=$ $n \sin i_{1}$ for small angles $i_{1}$ and $i_{2}$ becomes $n^{\prime} i_{2}=n i_{1}$. As $i_{3}=i_{1}, i_{3}=2 i_{2}$, we have $i_{1}=2 i_{2}$. Therefore, $n^{\prime}=2 n=\frac{8}{3}$.

\section{7}

A ray of light enters a spherical drop of water of index $n$ as shown (Fig. 1.21).

(1) What is the angle of incidence $\alpha$ of the ray on the back surface? Will this ray be totally or partially reflected?

(2) Find an expression for the angle of deflection $\delta$.

(3) Find the angle $\phi$ which produces minimum deflection.

$$
\left(\text { Hint : } \frac{d \sin ^{-1} x}{d x}=\frac{1}{\sqrt{1-x^{2}}}\right) \text {. }
$$



![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-030.jpg?height=238&width=514&top_left_y=212&top_left_x=231)

Fig. $1.21$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-030.jpg?height=213&width=497&top_left_y=228&top_left_x=841)

Fig. $1.22$

\section{Solution:}

(1) Figure $1.22$ shows the angles $\phi, \alpha, \phi-\alpha, x, \delta, \alpha$ is given by Snell's law $n \sin \alpha=\sin \phi$. Since $\sin \alpha=\frac{1}{n} \sin \phi<\frac{1}{n}$, i.e., the angle of incidence $\alpha$ is smaller than the critical angle $\sin ^{-1} \frac{1}{n}$, the incident light is reflected partially at the back spherical surface.

(2) As $\alpha=(\phi-\alpha)+x$, or $x=2 \alpha-\phi$, we have $\delta=\pi-2 x=\pi-4 \alpha+2 \phi$.

(3) For minimum deflection, we require $\frac{d \delta}{d \phi}=-4 \frac{d \alpha}{d \phi}+2=0$, or $\frac{d \alpha}{d \phi}=\frac{1}{2}$. As

$$
\alpha=\sin ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{n} \sin \phi\right),
$$

we have $\frac{d \alpha}{d \phi}=\frac{1}{n} \frac{\cos \phi}{\cos \alpha}$ and the above gives

$$
1-\frac{1}{n^{2}} \sin ^{2} \phi=\frac{4}{n^{2}} \cos ^{2} \phi,
$$

or

$$
1=\frac{1}{n^{2}}+\frac{3}{n^{2}} \cos ^{2} \phi,
$$

giving

$$
\cos ^{2} \phi=\frac{n^{2}-1}{3} .
$$

\section{8}

It was once suggested that the mirror for an astronomical telescope could be produced by rotating a flat disk of mercury at a prescribed angular velocity $\omega$ about a vertical axis.

(a) What is the equation of the reflection (free) surface so obtained?

(b) How fast must the disk be rotated to produce a $10 \mathrm{~cm}$ focal length mirror?

(Wisconsin) 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-031.jpg?height=336&width=447&top_left_y=197&top_left_x=477)

Fig. $1.28$

\section{Solution:}

(a) Owing to the symmetry of the reflecting surface, we need only to consider the vituation in a meridian plane. Let the equation of the reflecting surface be represented by (Fig. 1.23)

$$
z=f(r) \text {. }
$$

From dynamical considerations we have for a volume element $d V$

$$
\begin{gathered}
S \cos \theta=\rho g d V \\
S \sin \theta=\rho \omega^{2} r d V
\end{gathered}
$$

where $\rho$ and $S$ represent the density of mercury and the supporting force on $d V$ respectively. Thus

$$
\tan \theta=\frac{\omega^{2} r}{g} .
$$

As

$$
\tan \theta=\frac{d z}{d r},
$$

we obtain the differential equation for the reflecting surface,

$$
d z=\frac{\omega^{2} r d r}{g} .
$$

Integrating and putting $z=0$ for $r=0$, we have

$$
z=\frac{\omega^{2} r^{2}}{2 g} .
$$

For s spherical surface of radius $R$

$$
z=R-\sqrt{R^{2}-r^{2}} \approx \frac{r^{2}}{2 R}
$$

if $r<R$. The focal length of the rotating mercury surface is then

$$
f=\frac{R}{2}=\frac{g}{2 \omega^{2}} .
$$

For $f=10 \mathrm{~cm}$, we require $\omega=7 \mathrm{rad} / \mathrm{s}$.

\section{1}

Given two identical watch glasses glued together, the rear one silvered. Using autocollimation as sketched (Fig. 1.26), sharp focus is obtained for $L=20 \mathrm{~cm}$. Find $L$ for sharp focus when the space between the glasses is subsequently flled with water, $n=\frac{1}{3}$.

(Wisconsin) 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-034.jpg?height=327&width=577&top_left_y=232&top_left_x=476)

Fig. $1.26$

\section{Solution:}

With air between the glasses, only the silvered watch glass reflects and converges the rays to form an image, i.e., the system acts as a concave mirror. The formula for a concave mirror

$$
\frac{1}{u}+\frac{1}{v}=\frac{2}{r}
$$

gives for $u=v=20 \mathrm{~cm}, r=20 \mathrm{~cm}$.

With water between the glasses, the incident light is refracted twice at $A$ and reflected once at $B$ before forming the final image. Note that the first image formed by $A$ falls behind the mirror $B$ and becomes a virtual object to $B$. Similarly the image formed by $B$ is a virtual object to $A$. We therefore have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{1}{L}+\frac{n}{x} &=\frac{n-1}{20}, \\
-\frac{1}{x}+\frac{1}{y} &=\frac{1}{10}, \\
-\frac{n}{y}+\frac{1}{L} &=\frac{n-1}{20},
\end{aligned}
$$

which yield $L=12 \mathrm{~cm}$.

Thus, a sharp image will be formed at $L=12 \mathrm{~cm}$.

\section{2}

An object is placed $10 \mathrm{~cm}$ in front of a convering lens of focal length $10 \mathrm{~cm}$. A diverging lens of focal length $-15 \mathrm{~cm}$ is placed $5 \mathrm{~cm}$ behind the converging lens (Fig. 1.27). Find the position, size and character of the final image.

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-035.jpg?height=266&width=535&top_left_y=400&top_left_x=129)

Fig. $1.27$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-035.jpg?height=295&width=549&top_left_y=402&top_left_x=721)

Fig. $1.28$

\section{Solution:}

For the first lens, the object distance is $u_{1}=10 \mathrm{~cm}$. As $f_{1}=10 \mathrm{~cm}$ also, the image distance is $v_{1}=\infty$. Then for the second lens, the object distance is $u_{2}=\infty$. As $f_{2}=-15 \mathrm{~cm}, v_{2}=-15 \mathrm{~cm}$. Hence, the image coincides with the object.

Now consider the size and character of the final image. The focal length of the combined lens system is $f=-\frac{L_{2} f_{2}}{\Delta}$, with $\Delta=d-f_{1}-f_{2}$. As $d=5 \mathrm{~cm}, f_{1}=10 \mathrm{~cm}, f_{2}=-15 \mathrm{~cm}$, we have $f=15 \mathrm{~cm}$; also, as shown in Fig. 1.28,

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\overline{\mathrm{F}_{1} \overline{\mathrm{F}}}=\frac{f_{1}^{2}}{\Delta}=10 \mathrm{~cm}, \\
&\overline{\mathrm{F}_{2}^{\prime} \mathrm{F}^{\prime}}=\frac{-f_{2}^{2}}{\Delta}=-22.5 \mathrm{~cm} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Using Newton's formula, $x x^{\prime}=f^{2}$, we have for $x=-10 \mathrm{~cm}$

$$
x^{\prime}=-22.5 \mathrm{~cm} \text {. }
$$

The negative sign indicates that the image is to the left of $F^{\prime}$. The magnification is $m=\frac{-1}{x}=1.5$. Then the image is upright, virtual and magnified $1.5$ times. 

\section{8}

As shown in Fig. 1.29, the image which would be cast by the converging lens alone has a distance of $0.5 \mathrm{~cm}$ between top and bottom. Calculate the position and size of the final image. Draw a ray diagram showing image formation for a point on the image not on the axis of the lenses. Using at least 2 rays. (The rays need not be the same for both lenses.) Fig, $1.29$

\section{Solution:}

An object infinite distance away finds its image on the back focal plane of the converging lens, i.e., at $20-15=5 \mathrm{~cm}$ behind the diverging lens. The Gaussian lens formula

$$
\frac{1}{u}+\frac{1}{v}=\frac{1}{f}
$$

applied to the diverging lens then gives for $u=-5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $f=-10 \mathrm{~cm}$, $v=10 \mathrm{~cm}$. The lateral magnification is

$$
m=\frac{h}{h^{\prime}}=\frac{10}{5}=2 .
$$

Hence the size of the final image is

$$
0.5 \times 2=1 \mathrm{~cm} \text {. }
$$

\section{4}

Find and describe a combination of two converging lenses which produces an inverted, virtual image at the position of the object and of the same sise. By a sketch of image formation make it clear that your scheme will work.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

$L_{1}, L_{2}$ represent two converging lenses, and $f_{1}, u_{1}, v_{1}, f_{2}, u_{2}, v_{2}$ represent the focal lengths, object distances and image distances in the sequential formation of images, respectively, as shown in Fig. 1.31.

Consider $\mathrm{L}_{2}$ first. The object of $\mathrm{L}_{2}$ must be within the focal length to form a virtual image. If $B$, the image of $L_{1}$, or the object of $L_{2}$, is at a distance of $\frac{f_{2}}{2}$ in front of $L_{2}$, the final image $C$ would be at a distance of $v_{2}=-f_{2}$ in front of $L_{2}$, where the real object $A$ is placed, and would be twice as large as $B$.

Now, we have to insert $\mathrm{L}_{1}$ between $A$ and $B$ so that $C$, which is twice the sise of $B$, is as large as $A$. Thus $u_{1}=2 v_{1}$. Furthermore, as $f_{2}=u_{1}+v_{1}+u_{2}$ we obtain

$$
u_{1}=\frac{f_{2}}{3}, v_{1}=\frac{f_{2}}{6} .
$$

The Gaussian lens formula then gives $9 f_{1}=f_{2}$.

The converging lenses $L_{1}$ and $L_{2}$, with focal lengths $f_{2}=9 f_{1}$, and the object should be arranged as in Fig. 1.31.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-038.jpg?height=374&width=457&top_left_y=356&top_left_x=538)

Fig. 1.31

\section{5}

Two positive thin lenses $\mathrm{L}_{1}$ and $\mathrm{L}_{2}$ of equal focal length are separated by a distance of half their focal length (Fig, 1.32):

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-038.jpg?height=264&width=387&top_left_y=1056&top_left_x=571)

Fig. 1.32

(a) Locate the image position for an object placed at distance $4 f$ to the left of $\mathrm{L}_{1}$.

(b) Locate the focal points of this lens combination treated as a single thick lens.

(c) Locate the principal planes for this lens combination treated as a single thick lens.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Let $u_{1}, v_{1}, u_{2}, v_{2}$ represent object and image distances for $L_{1}$ and $\mathrm{L}_{2}$ respectively. As $u_{1}=4 f, \frac{1}{v_{1}}=\frac{1}{f_{1}}-\frac{1}{u_{1}}=\frac{3}{4 f}, v_{1}=\frac{4}{3} f$. Then as $u_{2}=\frac{l}{2}-v_{1}=-\frac{5}{6} f$, we have $\frac{1}{v_{2}}=\frac{1}{f}-\frac{1}{u_{2}}=\frac{11}{5 f}$ or $v_{2}=\frac{5 f}{11}$. Hence the final image is at $\frac{5 f}{11}$ right of $L_{2}$.

(b) Consider a beam of parallel light falling on $L$, from the left, then $v_{1}=f$ and

Therefore

$$
u_{2}=\frac{f}{2}-v_{1}=-\frac{f}{2} .
$$

$$
\frac{1}{v_{2}}=\frac{1}{f}+\frac{2}{f}=\frac{3}{f} \text {, or } v_{2}=\frac{1}{3} f \text {. }
$$

By symmetry, the two local points of the lens combination lie $\frac{1}{3}$ to the left of $L_{1}$ and $\frac{1}{3}$ to the right of $L_{2}$.

(c) On the principal planes, the lateral magnification $=1$. By symmetry, the image of an object on the left principal plane formed by $\mathrm{L}_{1}$ must coincide with the image of the same object on the right principal plane formed by $L_{2}$, and both must be at the mid-point between the two lenses.

Let the left principal plane be at $x$ left of $L_{1}$. Then $u_{1}=x, v_{1}=$ $\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)=\frac{1}{4} f$, and from $\frac{1}{f}=\frac{1}{u_{1}}+\frac{1}{v_{1}}$ we obtain $x=-\frac{1}{3} f$.

Therefore, the two principal planes are at $\frac{L}{3}$ to the right of $L_{1}$ and $\frac{d}{3}$ to the left of $\mathrm{L}_{2}$.

A self-luminous object of height $h$ is $40 \mathrm{~cm}$ to the left of a converging lens with a focal length of $10 \mathrm{~cm}$. A second converging lens with a focal length of $20 \mathrm{~cm}$ is $30 \mathrm{~cm}$ to the right of the first lens.

(a) Calculate the position of the final image.

(b) Calculate the ratio of the height of the final image to the height $h$ of the object.

(c) Draw a ray diagram, being careful to show just those rays needed to locate the final image starting from the self-luminous object.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) From $\frac{1}{f_{1}}=\frac{1}{u_{1}}+\frac{1}{v_{1}}$, where $f_{1}=10 \mathrm{~cm}, u_{1}=40 \mathrm{~cm}$, we obtain $v_{1}=13 \frac{1}{3} \mathrm{~cm}$.

From $\frac{1}{f_{2}}=\frac{1}{u_{2}}+\frac{1}{v_{2}}$, where $f_{2}=20 \mathrm{~cm}, u_{2}=\left(30-\frac{10}{3}\right) \mathrm{cm}$, we obtain $v_{2}=-100 \mathrm{~cm}$, i.e., the final image is $100 \mathrm{~cm}$ to the left of the second lens. (b) The ratio of the final image height to the height $h$ of the object can be calculated from

$$
m=m_{2} \times m_{1}=\left(\frac{v_{2}}{u_{2}}\right)\left(\frac{v_{1}}{u_{1}}\right)=-2 .
$$

The minus sign signifies an inverted image.

\section{7}

\section{arrive at the ray diagram.}

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-041.jpg?height=217&width=455&top_left_y=239&top_left_x=507)

Fig. $1.35$

Do all the refracting at planes 1 and 2.

(c) How would you change the position of the second (diverging) lens to make the combination a telephoto lens?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) As shown in Fig. 1.36, a ray would be totally reflected in the rod if

$$
\theta_{3}>\alpha=\sin ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{n}\right) \text {, }
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-041.jpg?height=280&width=230&top_left_y=919&top_left_x=611)

Fig. $1.36$

where $\alpha$ is the critical angle, $n$ is the index of refraction of the plastic, assuming the index of refraction of air to be unity. Thus we require that

or

$$
\theta_{2}=\frac{\pi}{2}-\theta_{3}<\frac{\pi}{2}-\sin ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{n}\right),
$$

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sin \theta_{1} &=n \sin \theta_{2}<n \sin \left(\frac{\pi}{2}-\sin ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{n}\right)\right) \\
&=n \cos \left(\sin ^{-1} \frac{1}{n}\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

As $\theta_{1} \leq \frac{\pi}{2}$, or $\sin \theta_{1} \leq 1$, we require $n \cos \left(\sin ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{n}\right)\right)>1$ for all rays to be totally reflected in the rod. Hence the condition for total reflection is

$$
\sin ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{n}\right)<\cos ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{n}\right) \text {, or } n>\sqrt{2}=1.414 .
$$

(b) First let us consider the effect of the first lens, $L_{1}$, alone (see Fig. 1.37). From A draw a ray parallel to the axis, which is refracted by $L_{1}$ and passes through $F_{1}^{\prime}$. Draw another ray from $A$ passing through $O_{1}$, the center of $L_{1}$. This ray is not refracted by $L_{1}$ and intersects the previous ray at $A^{\prime}$, forming the image. Draw $A^{\prime} F_{2}$ perpendicular to the axis. If $F_{2}$ is the focal point of $L_{2}$, the rays $A A^{\prime}$ and $F_{1}^{\prime} A^{\prime}$ will be refracted by $L_{2}$ and become parallel to $\mathrm{O}_{2} \mathrm{~A}^{\prime}$, which means that the final image will be at infinity.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-042.jpg?height=557&width=1186&top_left_y=545&top_left_x=144)

Fig. $1.37$

(c) To form a telephoto lens we place the second lens between the first lens and $F_{1}^{\prime}$ so that $F_{1}^{\prime}$ coincides with $F_{2}$. Note that we require $O_{2} F_{2}<O_{1} F_{1}^{\prime}$ (see Fig. 1.38). Rays entering the system with angular spread $\alpha$ will become rays with angular spread $\alpha^{\prime}$ with $\alpha^{\prime}>\alpha$ after passing through the optical system. Such a system is commonly known as the Galilean telescope.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-042.jpg?height=290&width=553&top_left_y=1409&top_left_x=489)

Fig. $1.38$

A thin, positive lens $L_{1}$ forms a real image of an object located very far way, as shown in Fig. 1.39. The image is located at a distance $4 l$ and has height $h$. (a) Find the distance from $L_{1}$ of the resultant image.
(b) Find the image height.

(Columbia)

\section{Solution:} we have

(a) Use the Gaussian lens formula $\frac{1}{u}+\frac{1}{v}=\frac{1}{f}$. Referring to Fig. $1.41$

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\mathrm{L}_{1}: v_{1}=4 l ; \\
&\mathrm{L}_{2}: f^{2}=-l, u_{2}=-(4-2) l=-2 l, \text { giving } v_{2}=-2 l ; \\
&\mathrm{L}_{3}: f_{3}=2 l, u_{3}=2 l+l=3 l \text {, giving } v_{3}=6 l .
\end{aligned}
$$

Hence, the distance from $\mathrm{L}_{1}$ of the resultant image is $3 l+6 l=9 l$.

\section{9}

For the combination of one prism and 2 lenses shown (Fig. 1.42), find the location and size of the final image when the object, length $1 \mathrm{~cm}$, is located as shown in the figure.

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-044.jpg?height=389&width=803&top_left_y=1229&top_left_x=358)

Fig. $1.42$

\section{Solution:}

For the right-angle prism, $n=1.5$, the critical angle $\alpha=\sin ^{-1}\left(\frac{1}{n}\right)=$ $42^{\circ}$, which is smaller than the angle of incidence, $45^{\circ}$, at the hypotenuse of the prism. Therefore total internal reflection occurs, which forms a virtual image. The prism, equivalent to a glass plate of thickness $6 \mathrm{~cm}$, would cause a image shift of

$$
\Delta L=6\left(1-\frac{1}{n}\right)=2 \mathrm{~cm} \text {. }
$$

Thus the effective object distance for the first lens is

$$
u_{1}=10+6+(6-2)=20 \mathrm{~cm} .
$$

As $u_{1}=f_{1}$, we have $v_{1}=\infty$. Then for the second lens, $u_{2}=\infty$. With $f_{2}=-10 \mathrm{~cm}$, we get

$$
v_{2}=f_{2}=-10 \mathrm{~cm} \text {. }
$$

Hence the final image is inverted and virtual, $10 \mathrm{~cm}$ to the left of the second lens.

The sise of the image is

$$
l_{2}=\left|\frac{f_{2}}{f_{1}}\right| \cdot 1=0.5 \mathrm{~cm} .
$$

\section{0}

A $35 \mathrm{~mm}$ camera lens of focal length $50 \mathrm{~mm}$ is made into a telephoto lens by placing a negative lens between it and the film, as shown (Fig. 1.43). $\mathrm{L}_{1}=$ camera lens, $f_{1}=50 \mathrm{~mm} ; \mathrm{L}_{2}=$ negative lens, $f_{2}=-100 \mathrm{~mm}$.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-045.jpg?height=345&width=502&top_left_y=1325&top_left_x=492)

Fig. $1.43$

(8) What is the distance $x$ if the system is focused at an object $50 \mathrm{~cm}$ in front of $\mathrm{L}_{1}$ ?

(b) What is the magnification produced by the lens combination?

(Wisconsin) 

\section{Solution:}

Let $u_{1}, v_{1}, f_{1}, u_{2}, v_{2}, f_{2}$ represent the object distances, image distances and focal lengths for lenses $L_{1}$ and $L_{2}$, respectively.

(a) $u_{1}=50 \mathrm{~cm}=500 \mathrm{~mm}$. The lens formula $\frac{1}{v}=\frac{1}{\mathrm{~J}}-\frac{1}{u}$ gives $v_{1}=\frac{500}{9}$ $\mathrm{mm}$ and $u_{2}=20 \mathrm{~mm}-v_{1}=-\frac{320}{9} \mathrm{~mm}$. It then gives

$$
\frac{1}{v_{2}}=\frac{1}{f_{2}}-\frac{1}{u_{2}}=\frac{29}{1600} \text {, or } x=v_{2}=55.2 \mathrm{~mm} \text {. }
$$

(b) The magnification of the lens combination is the product of that of each lens:

$$
\begin{aligned}
M &=\left|\frac{v_{1}}{u_{1}}\right| \times\left|\frac{v_{2}}{u_{2}}\right|=\frac{\left[\frac{(500 \times 1600)}{(9 \times 29)}\right]}{\left[\frac{(500 \times 320)}{9}\right]} \\
&=\frac{5}{29}=0.17 .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{1}

In a compound microscope the focal length of the objective is $0.5 \mathrm{~cm}$ and that of the eyepiece is $2 \mathrm{~cm}$. If the distance between lenses is $22 \mathrm{~cm}$, what should the distance from the object to the objective be if the observer focuses for image at $\infty$ ? What is the magnifying power? Answer within $10 \%$. Derive all necessary formulae from the lens equation $\frac{1}{p}+\frac{1}{q}=\frac{1}{f}$. Normal near point of eye is $15 \mathrm{~cm}$.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

Apply the lens equation $\frac{1}{p}+\frac{1}{q}=\frac{1}{f}$. For the eyepiece (subscript 2), $q_{2}=\infty$, we get $p_{2}=f_{2}=2 \mathrm{~cm}$.

For the objective (subscript 1),

$$
q_{1}=d-p_{2}=20 \mathrm{~cm},
$$

where $d$ is the distance between the lenses. With $f_{1}=0.5 \mathrm{~cm}$, we get $p_{1}=0.51 \mathrm{~cm}$. Hence, the object is located $0.51 \mathrm{~cm}$ in front of the objective.

To find the magnifying power, let the object length be $y$. The length of the image formed by the objective is

$$
y^{\prime}=\frac{q_{1}}{p_{1}} y .
$$

The angle subtended at the eye by the final image is then

$$
\alpha^{\prime}=\frac{y^{\prime}}{f_{2}}=\frac{q_{1}}{p_{1}} \frac{y}{f_{2}} .
$$

The angle subtended at the eye by the object without use of microscope is

$$
\alpha=\frac{y}{15} .
$$

Hence the magnifying power of the microscope is

$$
m=\frac{\alpha^{\prime}}{\alpha}=\frac{15 q_{1}}{p_{1} f_{2}}=2.9 \times 10^{2} .
$$

Consider the diagram as shown (Fig. 1.44). A slit source is to be imaged on a screen. The light is to be parallel between the lenses. The index of refraction of the prism is $n(\lambda)=1.5+0.02\left(\lambda-\lambda_{0}\right) / \lambda_{0}$, where $\lambda_{0}=5000 \AA$. System is aligned on $5000 \AA$ A light.

(a) What are the focal lengths of the lenses?

(b) What is the linear and angular magnification of the slit at the screen? Is the image inverted? Make a ray diagram.

(c) What is the displacement off axis of light from source of $\lambda=5050 \AA$ ? Approximate where possible.

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-047.jpg?height=405&width=671&top_left_y=1430&top_left_x=396)

Pig. 1.44 

\section{Solution:}

(a) As the light emerging from the first lens $L_{1}$ is parallel, the slit must be on the front focal plane of $L_{1}$, i.e.,

$$
f_{1}=25 \mathrm{~cm} .
$$
have

As the parallel light is imaged by the second lens $L_{2}$ on the screen, we

$$
f_{2}=10 \mathrm{~cm} .
$$

(b) Consider the diagram shown in Fig. 1.45, which represents a coaxial optical system equivalent to the original one. For a prism with small apex angle $(\varphi=0.1 \mathrm{rad})$ the angular deviation is very small, $\delta=\varphi(n-1)=$ $0.05 \mathrm{rad}$, and we can consider $d$, the distance between $L_{1}$ and $\mathrm{L}_{2}$, to be $\approx 10 \mathrm{~cm}$. The positions of the cardinal points of the combination can be calculated as follows,

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\Delta=d-f_{1}-f_{2}=-25 \mathrm{~cm}, \\
&f=-\frac{f_{1} f_{2}}{\Delta}=10 \mathrm{~cm} \\
&\overline{\mathbf{A}_{1} \mathrm{H}}=\frac{f d}{f^{2}}=10 \mathrm{~cm}, \\
&\overline{\mathrm{A}_{2} \mathrm{H}^{\prime}}=-\frac{f d}{f_{1}}=-4 \mathrm{~cm} \\
&\overline{\mathrm{F}_{1} \mathrm{~F}}=\frac{f_{1}^{2}}{\Delta}=-25 \mathrm{~cm} \\
&\overline{\mathrm{F}_{2}^{\prime} \mathrm{F}^{\prime}}=-\frac{f_{2}^{2}}{\Delta}=4 \mathrm{~cm} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Thus $F^{\prime}$ is $4 \mathrm{~cm}$ in front of the screen, i.e., $x^{\prime}=4 \mathrm{~cm}$, so the linear magnification is

$$
m=-\frac{x^{\prime}}{f}=-0.4
$$

The negative sign shows that the image is inverted. The ray diagram is shown in Fig. 1.45. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-049.jpg?height=495&width=1076&top_left_y=220&top_left_x=179)

Fig. $1.45$

The angular magnification, which is the ratio of the tangents of the slopes of conjugate rays to the axis, is given by

$$
M=\frac{-1}{f+x^{\prime}}\left(\frac{1}{f+x}\right)^{-1}=-2.5 \text {. }
$$

(c) The angular derivation $\delta$ is a function of wavelength $\lambda$ through

Hence

$$
\delta=[n(\lambda)-1] \varphi .
$$

$$
\Delta \delta=\varphi \frac{d n}{d \lambda} \Delta \lambda=0.1 \times \frac{0.02}{5000} \times(5050-5000)=2 \times 10^{-5} .
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-049.jpg?height=296&width=664&top_left_y=1554&top_left_x=379)

Fig. $1.46$ Consider the following simple telescope configuration made up of thin lenses as follows:

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\mathrm{L}_{1}: f_{1}=10 \mathrm{~cm}, \text { Diameter }=4 \mathrm{~cm} \\
&\mathrm{~L}_{2}: f_{2}=2 \mathrm{~cm}, D=1.2 \mathrm{~cm} \\
&\mathrm{~L}_{3}: f_{3}=2 \mathrm{~cm}, D=1.2 \mathrm{~cm}
\end{aligned}
$$

(a) Trace a typical bundle of rays through the system. exit pupil.

(b) Calculate the position of the exit pupil and the diameter of the

(c) What is the function of the lens $\mathrm{L}_{2}$ ?

(d) Is the instrument a good match to the eye? (Explain).

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Let us consider a bundle of parallel rays falling normally on $L_{1}$ as shown in Fig. 1.47. We are given that the back focal point of $L_{1}$ and the front focal point of $L_{3}$ coincide with the center of $L_{2}$, so the bundle of parallel rays suffers no refraction at $\mathrm{L}_{2}$.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-050.jpg?height=252&width=499&top_left_y=993&top_left_x=509)

Fig. 1.47

(b) The lens $\mathrm{L}_{1}$ functions as the aperture and the entrance pupil of the telescope. The image of the aperture of $L_{1}$ formed by the following lenses is the exit pupil of the telescope, the position of which can be calculated using the lens formula twice:

$$
\begin{aligned}
&\frac{1}{S_{2}}+\frac{1}{S_{2}^{\prime}}=\frac{1}{f_{2}}, \\
&\frac{1}{S_{3}}+\frac{1}{S_{3}^{\prime}}=\frac{1}{f_{3}},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $S_{2}=10 \mathrm{~cm}, f_{2}=f_{3}=2 \mathrm{~cm}$.

Solving the two equations in turn, we have

$$
S_{2}^{\prime}=2.5 \mathrm{~cm}, S_{3}=2-2.5=-0.5 \mathrm{~cm},
$$

and hence 

$$
S_{3}^{\prime}=0.4 \mathrm{~cm},
$$

i.e., the exit pupil is located $0.4 \mathrm{~cm}$ behind $\mathrm{L}_{3}$. From the similar triangles we have $\frac{D^{\prime}}{D}=\frac{f_{3}}{f_{1}}$, so that the diameter of the exit pupil is

$$
D^{\prime}=\frac{f_{3}}{f_{1}} D=0.8 \mathrm{~cm} .
$$

(c) The lens $L_{2}$ placed at the front focal point of $L_{3}$ is called the field lens and serves as the field stop in the configuration. Its function is to converge the rays before they enter the eye lens $L_{3}$, so that one can get a comfortable image with a smaller eye lens. Furthermore, the use of a system of $L_{2}$ and $L_{3}$ of the same glass separated by $\left(f_{2}+f_{3}\right) / 2$, which has the same focal length for all colors, as the ocular eliminates lateral chromatic observation.

(d) In addition to having a large aperture objective to give the necessary resolving power, the objects will only be resolved if the magnification is sufficiently large to present the necessary separation to the eye. For this purpose the minimum magnification required is

$$
m_{0}=\frac{60}{\frac{140}{D}},
$$

where $D$ is in centimeters. However, for comfort of the eye the actual magnification should be $1.5$ to 2 times the minimum, i.e.,

$$
m=\frac{120 D}{140}=3.4
$$

with $D=4 \mathrm{~cm}$ as given.

The magnification for the given system is

$$
\frac{f_{1}}{f_{3}}=5>m .
$$

Hence, as far as magnification is concerned the system is quite up to the standard. On the other hand, for a good optical system, the exit pupil should be designed to coincide with the eye and the distance between the exit pupil and the last optical surface of the eye lens should not be less than $5 \mathrm{~mm}$. Such distance for this system is only $4 \mathrm{~mm}$, which would make the eyelash touch the optical surface. Moreover, the size of the exit pupil of a telescope should be as large as the eye, i.e., $2-4 \mathrm{~mm}$. The diameter of the exit pupil of this system is $8 \mathrm{~mm}$, so the light emerging from the telescope will not all enter the eye.

For these reasons, the telescope is not a good match to the eye.

1084

Two telescopes have the same objective lens with focal length $f_{0}$. One telescope has a converging lens for an eyepiece with focal length $f_{c}$. The other has a diverging lens for an eyepiece with focal length $-f_{\mathrm{d}}$. The magnification for these two telescopes is the same for objects at infinity. What is the ratio of the lengths of these two telescopes in terms of the magnification $M$ ? Give a good reason why one might choose to use the longer telescope for a given $M$.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

The magnification, usually the angular magnification, of a visual instrument is defined as the ratio of the angles $\omega^{\prime}$ and $\omega$ which are made by the chief rays from the top of the object with the axis at the aided and unaided eyes respectively:

\section{For a telescope}

$$
M=\frac{\omega^{\prime}}{\omega} .
$$

$$
M=-\frac{f_{0}}{f_{\mathrm{e}}} .
$$

where $f_{0}$ and $f_{\mathrm{e}}$ are the focal lengths of the objective lens and the eyepiece respectively. A telescope system uses a converging or a diverging lens as the eyepiece. A telescope with converging eyepiece is called a Keplerian telescope, while that with diverging eyepiece is called a Galilean telescope. For the Keplerian and Galilean telescopes given the magnifications are

and

$$
M_{\mathrm{K}}=-\frac{f_{0}}{f_{\mathrm{c}}}
$$

$$
M_{\mathbf{G}}=\frac{-f_{0}}{-f_{\mathrm{d}}}=\frac{f_{0}}{f_{\mathrm{d}}}
$$

respectively, where the minus sign corresponds to an inverted image and the plus an erect one. We are given that

$$
-M_{\mathrm{K}}=M_{\mathrm{G}}=M \text {, }
$$

hence 

$$
f_{\mathrm{c}}=f_{\mathrm{d}} .
$$

For a telescope, the secondary focal point of the objective coincides with the primary focal point of the eyepiece. Thus the length of a telescope is $L=f_{0}+f_{e}$.

For the two telescopes given, we have

$$
L_{\mathrm{K}}=f_{0}+f_{\mathrm{c}}, \quad L_{\mathrm{G}}=f_{0}-f_{\mathrm{d}} .
$$

Obviously,

$$
\begin{aligned}
&L_{\mathrm{K}}>L_{\mathrm{G}}, \\
&\frac{L_{\mathrm{K}}}{L_{\mathrm{G}}}=\frac{\left(f_{0}+f_{c}\right)}{\left(f_{0}-f_{d}\right)}=\frac{\left(\left(f_{0} / f_{\mathrm{c}}\right)+1\right)}{\left(\left(f_{0} / f_{\mathrm{d}}\right)-1\right)}=\frac{(M+1)}{(M-1)} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Comparing with the Galilean telescope, the advantages of the Keplerian telescope are as follows:

(a) Between the objective lens and the eyepiece, there is a real image plane where a graticule can be placed as reference mark for making measurements.

(b) A field stop can be placed in the image plane, so that the entrance window overlaps the object plane at infinity. This makes a higher quality image with a larger field of view and no vignetting.

However, the Keplerian telescope has the disadvantage that it forms an inverted image, which is inconvenient for the observer. Adding an imageinverter could eliminate this defect. Therefore, although the length of a Keplerian telescope is longer than that of a Galilean one for the same magnification, the former is still to be preferred to the latter.

\section{5}

(a) Derive the lens makers' formula for a thin lens

$$
\frac{1}{f}=(n-1)\left(\frac{1}{R}+\frac{1}{R^{\prime}}\right) .
$$

(b) Crown and flint glass lenses are cemented together to form an achromatic lens. Show that the focal lengths of the crown and tint components satisfy the equation

where

$$
\frac{\Delta_{\mathrm{c}}}{f_{\mathrm{c}}}+\frac{\Delta_{\mathrm{f}}}{f_{\mathrm{f}}}=0
$$



$$
\Delta=\frac{1}{\bar{n}-1} \frac{d n}{d \lambda} \Delta \lambda
$$

and $\bar{n}$ is the value of the index of refraction of the glass at the center of the visible spectrum and $\Delta \lambda$ the wavelength spread $(\sim 3000 \AA)$ of the visible spectrum.

(c) For crown glass $\Delta_{c}=0.0169$ and for flint glass $\Delta_{i}=0.0384$. Show that one must use a converging crown glass lens cemented to a diverging flint glass lens to get a converging achromatic lens.

(Columbia)

Solution:

(a) Please refer to any standard textbook.

(b) For the compound lens formed by cementing the two component lenses together, the focal length $f$ satisfies the equation

$$
\frac{1}{f}=\frac{1}{f_{c}}+\frac{1}{f_{t}} .
$$

Taking derivatives with respect to $\lambda$ yields

$$
\frac{1}{f^{2}} \frac{d f}{d \lambda}=\frac{1}{f_{c}^{2}}\left(\frac{d f_{\mathrm{c}}}{d \lambda}\right)+\frac{1}{f_{f}^{2}}\left(\frac{d f_{\mathrm{f}}}{d \lambda}\right) .
$$

Differentiating the lens makers' formula

$$
\frac{1}{f}=(n-1)\left(\frac{1}{R}+\frac{1}{R^{\prime}}\right),
$$

we have

$$
\frac{1}{f^{2}} \frac{d f}{d \lambda}=\left(\frac{1}{R}+\frac{1}{R^{\prime}}\right) \frac{d r}{d \lambda},
$$

or, making use of (3),

or,

$$
\frac{1}{f} \frac{d f}{d \lambda}=\frac{1}{\bar{n}-1} \frac{d n}{d \lambda}=\frac{\Delta}{\Delta \lambda},
$$

So we have

$$
\frac{1}{f^{2}} \frac{d f}{d \lambda}=\frac{\Delta}{\Delta \lambda} \frac{1}{f} .
$$

and

$$
\frac{1}{f_{\mathrm{c}}^{2}} \frac{d f_{\mathrm{c}}}{d \lambda}=\frac{\Delta_{\mathrm{c}}}{\Delta \lambda} \frac{1}{f_{\mathrm{c}}}
$$



$$
\frac{1}{f_{f}^{2}} \frac{d f_{f}}{d \lambda}=\frac{\Delta_{f}}{\Delta \lambda} \frac{1}{f_{f}} .
$$

Substituting these expressions into (2) and noting that $\frac{d f}{d \lambda}=0$ for an achromatic lens, we have

$$
\frac{\Delta_{c}}{f_{c}}+\frac{\Delta_{\mathrm{l}}}{f_{\mathrm{f}}}=0 .
$$

(c) Solving (1) and (4), we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{1}{f_{c}} &=\frac{\Delta_{l}}{f\left(\Delta_{f}-\Delta_{c}\right)}, \\
\frac{1}{f_{l}} &=\frac{-\Delta_{c}}{f\left(\Delta_{t}-\Delta_{c}\right)} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Given that $\Delta_{c}<\Delta_{t}$ and the requirement $f>0$, we have

$$
f_{c}>0 \text { and } f_{t}<0 \text {. }
$$

Therefore, to get a converging achromatic lens one must use a converging crown glass lens cemented to a diverging flint glass lens.

\section{6}

A common piece of optical equipment is the viewgraph machine used in physics colloquia and seminars. A rough sketch is shown below (Fig. 1.48).

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-055.jpg?height=281&width=495&top_left_y=1308&top_left_x=443)

Fig. $1.48$

One or more optical elements are located at positions $\mathbf{A}, \mathbf{B}, \mathbf{C}$, and D. Describe each element and explain how the elements are used in the operation of the viewgraph machine. Write down the relations which must be satisfied by the distances between the elements. Which element in this device was not available at reasonable cost in $1900 ?$

(Princeton) 

\section{Solution:}

There are two types of viewgraph machines, transmission and reflection. The equipment shown belongs to the former type.

$A$ is the illumination system and consists of a lamp and a couple of condensing lenses. $B$ is a transparency to be projected. C is the image formation and projection system consisting of an objective lens and a mirror. $D$ is the screen.

The light, emitted by the lamp and condensed by the condensing lenses, illuminates the "object" - the transpareney, on which there are words or drawings. The object is imaged by the objective lens and then reflected and projected by the mirror onto the screen to form an enlarged image.

The objective lens is the key element, upon which the quality of the resultant image depends. The objective lens has to be specially designed and manufactured in order to eliminate, as much as possible, spherical and chromatic aberrations, astigmatism and field curvature. Sometimes one even employs a camera objective lens for this purpose. So the objective must have been very expensive in 1900.

The image plane of the source formed by the condensing lenses should coincide with the pupil of the objective lens. That is to say, the lamp and the pupil of the objective lens are conjugate for the condenser. The transparency and the screen should also be a pair of conjugate object and image for the objective lens. The distance between the transparency and the objective lens, the distance between the objective lens and the screen (including the distance from the objective lens to the center of the mirror and that from the center of the mirror to the screen) and the focal length of the objective lens should satisfy the lens makers' formula.

\section{7}

A lens (of focal length $f$ ) gives the image of the sun on its focal plane. Prove that the brightness of the image $\left(W / \mathrm{cm}^{2}\right)$ is near that of the surface of the sun.

(Columbia)

\section{Solution:}

The sun may be considered a Lambert source with apparent luminance, or brightness, $L$ and area $S$. The power radiated into the lens (of area $A$ and focal length $f$ ) is

$$
\Phi=\frac{L S A}{R^{2}},
$$

where $R$ is the distance between the sun and the earth. All of the power collected is directed through $S^{\prime}$ (the image of $S$ ).

The apparent luminance $L^{\prime}$ of the image is

$$
\boldsymbol{\Phi}^{\prime}=L^{\prime} S^{\prime} d \Omega^{\prime},
$$

where $d \Omega^{\prime}$ is the solid angle subtended by the lens at the image, i.e.,

$$
d \Omega^{\prime}=\frac{A}{f^{2}} .
$$

If we neglect attenuation in the atmosphere and the lens, then $\Phi \approx \Phi^{\prime}$, i.e.,

$$
\frac{L S A}{R^{2}} \approx \frac{L^{\prime} A S^{\prime}}{f^{2}} .
$$

As $S / R^{2}=S^{\prime} / f^{2}$, we have

$$
L \approx L^{\prime} .
$$

\section{8}

(It is impossible to increase the apparent brightness of an extended (large solid angle) diffuse light source with lenses. This problem illustrates that fact for a single lens.) A light source of brightness $S$ subtends a solid angle that is larger than the acceptance solid angle $\Omega$ of a telescope that observes it. The source emits $S$ units of optical energy per unit area per unit solid angle per second isotropically. The objective lens of the telescope has area $A$ and is a thin lens.

(a) Show that the energy entering the telescope per second is $S \Omega A$.

(b) Show that the product of the area of the image formed by the objective lens and the solid angle subtended by the objective lens at the image i $\Omega A$.

(c) Explain why the above results show that the apparent brightness of the extended source is not changed by the objective lens of the telescope.

(UC, Berkeley)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The light flux entering the telescope per second is given by

$$
\phi=\int\left(S d \Sigma d \Sigma^{\prime} \cos \theta \cos ^{\prime} / r^{2}\right)
$$

where $d \Sigma$ is an area element of the source surface and $d \Sigma^{\prime}$ is an area element of the telescope lens, $\theta$ is the angle which the telescope axis makes with the normal to $d \Sigma, \theta^{\prime}$ is the angle which the axis makes with the normal to $d \Sigma^{\prime}, r$ is the distance between $d \Sigma$ and $d \Sigma^{\prime}$. As $\int\left(d \Sigma / r^{2}\right)$, the solid angle subtended by the source, is larger than the acceptance solid angle $\cap$ of the telescope, only light emitted from an effective area $\Omega r^{2}$ enters into the telescope, i.e., $\int\left(d \Sigma / r^{2}\right)=\Omega$. Then as $\int d \Sigma^{\prime}=A$, we have

$$
\Phi=S \Omega A \text {. }
$$

Here we have assumed that the distance between the source and the telescope is very large and that the telescope observes directly, so that both $\cos \theta$ and $\cos \theta^{\prime}$ are each equal to unity.

(b) The area of the image, $\sigma^{\prime}$, is given by

$$
\sigma^{\prime}=\Omega f^{2},
$$

where $f$ is the focal length of the objective lens of the telescope. The solid angle $\Omega^{\prime}$ subtended by the lens at the image is

$$
\Omega^{\prime}=\left(\frac{\pi D^{2}}{4}\right) / f^{2},
$$

where $D$ is the diameter of the lens. Therefore we have

$$
\sigma^{\prime} \Omega^{\prime}=\frac{\pi D^{2}}{4 f^{2}} \cdot \Omega f^{2}=\frac{\pi D^{2}}{4} \cdot \Omega=A \cdot \Omega .
$$

(c) The light flux in the image area is

$$
\Phi^{\prime}=\pi S^{\prime} \sigma^{\prime} \sin ^{2} u^{\prime},
$$

where $S^{\prime}$ is the brightness of the image, $u^{\prime}$ is the angular semi-aperture of the lens, given by $\sin u^{\prime}=\frac{D}{2 J}$. Hence

$$
\Phi^{\prime}=\pi S^{\prime}\left(\Omega f^{2}\right)\left(\frac{D}{2 f}\right)^{2}=S^{\prime} \Omega A \text {. }
$$

As the transmission coefficient of the lens is smaller than unity, i.e., $\Phi^{\prime} \leq \Phi$, we get $S^{\prime} \leq S$. That is, it is impossible to increase the apparent brightness of an extended diffuse source with a telescope. 

\section{9}

When the sun is overhead, a flat white surface has a certain luminous flux. A lens of radius $r$ and focal length $f$ is now used to focus the sun's image on the sheet. How much greater is the flux in the image area? For a given $r$, what must $f$ be so that the lens gives no increase in flux in the image? From the earth the diameter of the sun subtends about $0.01$ radians. The only light in the image is through the lens.

\section{(UC, Berkeley)}

\section{Solution:}

The luminous flux reaching the earth is given by

$$
\boldsymbol{\Phi}=B \sigma^{\prime} d \Omega
$$

where $B$ is the brightness of the sun, regarded as a Lambert radiator, $\sigma^{\prime}$ is the area illuminated on the earth, $d \Omega$ is the solid angle subtended by the sun at $\sigma^{\prime}$ and is given by

$$
d \Omega=\pi \alpha^{2},
$$

$\alpha$ being the angular aperture of the sun, equal to about $0.01$ radians.

The luminous flux on the image area behind the lens is

$$
\Phi^{\prime}=\pi B \sigma^{\prime} \sin ^{2} u^{\prime}=\pi B \sigma^{\prime}\left(\frac{r}{f}\right)^{2}
$$

where $u^{\prime}$ is the semi-angular aperture of the lens, $r$ and $f$ are the radius and focal length of the lens respectively. Consider the ratio

$$
\frac{\Phi^{\prime}}{\Phi}=\frac{\pi B \sigma^{\prime}\left(\frac{r^{2}}{f^{2}}\right)}{\pi B \sigma^{\prime}(0.01)^{2}}=\frac{10^{4} r^{2}}{f^{2}},
$$

where we have taken the transparency of the lens glass to be unity. Thus the luminous flux on the image area is $10^{4} r^{2} / f^{2}$ times that on a surface of the same ares on the earth without the lens.

For $\Phi^{\prime} / \Phi \leq 1$, we have $f \geq 10^{2}$ r. Hence, for $f \geq 100$ r the lens will give no increase in flux in the image area.

1040

(a) Three identical positive lenses, of focal length $f$, are aligned and separated by a distance $f$ from each other, as shown in Fig. 1.49. An object is located $f / 2$ in front of the leftmost lens. Find the position and the magnification power of the resultant image by using tracing method.

(b) Looking through a small hole is a wellknown method to improve sight. If your eyes are near-sighted and can focus an object $20 \mathrm{~cm}$ away without using any glasses, estimate the required diameter of the hole through which you would have good sight for objects far away.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-060.jpg?height=215&width=501&top_left_y=492&top_left_x=541)

(Columbia)

Fig. $1.49$

\section{Solution:}

(a) We construct the image using two rays passing through the top of the object, one parallel to the axis, the other through the front focal point of $L_{1}$, as shown in Fig. 1.50. The resultant image is found at a distance $f / 2$ behind the rightmost lens. The magnification is $-1$.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-060.jpg?height=255&width=571&top_left_y=975&top_left_x=506)

Fis. $1.50$

(b) Inside the human eye the distance between the crystalline lens and the retina is $20 \mathrm{~mm}$. Using the lens formula $\frac{1}{u}+\frac{1}{v}=\frac{1}{f}$, we get $f=19 \mathrm{~cm}$ for the image distance $2.0 \mathrm{~cm}$ and the object $20 \mathrm{~cm}$ away.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-060.jpg?height=175&width=452&top_left_y=1417&top_left_x=557)

Fig. $1.61$

As Fig. $1.51$ shows, a lens focuses an object infinitely far away at the focal point $F$ and forms a disk $A B$ on the retina $1.0 \mathrm{~cm}$ behind $F$. An opening placed in front of the eye lens determines the diameter of the beam of light entering the eye. From the geometry, $d=\overline{\mathrm{CD}}=19 \overline{\mathrm{AB}}$. On account of the resolving angular limitation of the eye, which is about $1^{\prime}=3 \times 10^{-4} \mathrm{rad}$, we get a transverse resolving limitation of $\overline{\mathrm{AB}}_{\mathrm{m}}=3 \times 10^{-4} \times 20=6 \times 10^{-3}$ $\mathrm{mm}$. If the diameter of the opening $d<19 \times 6 \times 10^{-3}=0.12 \mathrm{~mm}$, we would have good sight for objects far away.

\section{1}

One side of a disk, $1 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ area, radiates uniformly and isotropically (a Lambert's law radiator) with a brightness of 1 watt $\cdot \mathrm{cm}^{-2}$ steradian ${ }^{-1}$ at a single frequency in the visible. the disk?

(a) What is the total rate at which energy is radiated from this face of

(b) Given a fused quarts lens $(n=1.5)$ whose diameter is $10 \mathrm{~cm}$ and whose focal length is $100 \mathrm{~cm}$, show how to image the radiator onto a disk whose area is $\mathrm{cm}^{2}$.

(c) Detimate the total energy fux reaching the $1 \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ disk to within a few percent.

(d) By changing $n$ and the dimensions of the lens, you may increase the energy flux reaching the $\frac{1}{4} \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ disk. By what reasoning might you determine the maximum energy fux into the $\frac{1}{4} \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ disk that can be achieved?

(CUSPEA)

\section{Solution:}

(a) $\Phi=B S \int_{0}^{2 \pi} d \varphi \int_{0}^{\pi} \sin \theta \cos \theta d \theta=\pi B S=3.14 \mathrm{~W}$, where $B$ is the brightness of the source, $S$ the area of the source, $\theta$ the angle between the direction of the emitted light and the normal to the surface of the source.

(b) The magnification is given by

$$
m=\frac{D^{\prime}}{D}=\left(\frac{S^{\prime}}{S}\right)^{\frac{1}{3}}=0.5
$$

where $D^{\prime}, D, S^{\prime}$ and $S$ are the diameters and the areas of the image and the object respectively. As $m=\frac{v}{u}=0.5$, we have $u=2 v$.

The lens equation then gives $u=3 f=300 \mathrm{~cm}, v=\frac{3 f}{2}=150 \mathrm{~cm}$. Therefore, if the object and image distances are $300 \mathrm{~cm}$ and $150 \mathrm{~cm}$ respectively, the lens images the source onto a disk of area $0.25 \mathrm{~cm}$.

(c) If the lens does not cause attenuation, the energy flux reaching the $\frac{1}{4} \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ disk would be equal to that reaching the lens. The solid angle subtended by the lens at the source is

$$
n=\frac{\pi r^{2}}{u^{2}} .
$$

The total energy flux reaching the lens is then

$$
\Phi^{\prime}=B S \Omega=1 \times 1 \times \pi \frac{5^{2}}{300^{2}}=8.7 \times 10^{-4} \mathrm{~W} .
$$

(d) According to the second law of thermodynamics, the brightness of the image cannot exceed that of the object. So the maximum energy flux into the $\frac{1}{4} \mathrm{~cm}^{2}$ disk is

$$
\Phi^{\prime \prime}=\frac{\Phi S^{\prime}}{S}=\frac{\Phi}{4}=0.785 \mathrm{~W} .
$$



\section{PART 2 WAVE OPTICS}

The width of a certain spectral line at $500 \mathrm{~nm}$ is $2 \times 10^{-2} \mathrm{~nm}$. Approximately what is the largest path difference for which interference fringes produced by this light are clearly visible?

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

The coherence length $l_{c}$ is given by

$$
. \lambda_{c}=\frac{\lambda^{2}}{\Delta \lambda}=1.25 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~cm} .
$$

If the optical path difference is about a quarter of $l_{c}, 3 \times 10^{-4} \mathrm{~cm}$, we can observe the fringes clearly.

\section{2}

A point source $S$ located at the origin of a coordinate system emits a spherical sinusoidal wave in which the electric field $E_{1}$ is given by $E_{1}=$ $A\left(\frac{D}{r}\right) \cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi r}{\lambda}\right)$, where $r$ is the distance from $S$. In addition, there is a plane wave propagating along the $x$-axis. This wave is given by

$$
E_{2}=A \cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi x}{\lambda}\right) .
$$

(Note that we treat both $E_{1}$ and $E_{2}$ as scalar waves in this problem.) Both waves are incident on a flat screen perpendicular to the $x$-2xis and at a distance $D$ from the origin, as shown in the figure (Fig. 2.1). Compute the resultant intensity $I$ at the screen as a function of the distance $y$ from the $x$-axis for values of $y$ small compared with $D$. Express $I$ in terms of $y, D, \lambda$ and the intensity $I_{0}$ at $y=0$.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-065.jpg?height=357&width=492&top_left_y=1499&top_left_x=463)

(Wisconsin)

Fig. $2.1$ Solution:

The electric field $E$ at a point, distance $r$ from the origin, on the screen is given by

$$
\begin{aligned}
E &=E_{1}+E_{2}=A\left[\frac{D}{r} \cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi r}{\lambda}\right)+\cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi x}{\lambda}\right)\right] \\
& \approx A\left[\cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi r}{\lambda}\right)+\cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi x}{\lambda}\right)\right]
\end{aligned}
$$

where we have used the approximation $r \approx D$.

For $y<D$, we have

$$
r=\left(D^{2}+y^{2}\right)^{\frac{1}{2}} \approx D\left(1+\frac{y^{2}}{2 D^{2}}\right)
$$

and thus

Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
E &=A\left[\cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi D\left(1+\frac{y^{2}}{2 D^{2}}\right)}{\lambda}\right)+\cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi D}{\lambda}\right)\right] \\
&=2 A \cos \frac{\pi y^{2}}{D \lambda} \cdot \cos \left(\omega t-\frac{2 \pi D\left(1+\frac{y^{2}}{4 D^{2}}\right)}{\lambda}\right) .
\end{aligned}
$$

$$
I \propto E^{2} \propto \cos ^{2} \frac{\pi y^{2}}{D \lambda} .
$$

Let $I=I_{0}$ at $y=0$; we then have

$$
I=I_{0} \cos ^{2}\left(\frac{\pi y^{2}}{D \lambda}\right) .
$$

As the distance from the centre increases, the fringes become closer. This is similar to Fresnel's zone plate.

\section{8}

A two-slit Young's interference experiment is arranged as illustrated (Fig. 2.2); $\lambda=5000 \AA$. When a thin film of a transparent material is put behind one of the slits, the zero order fringe moves to the position previously occupied by the 4th order bright fringe. The index of refraction of the film is $n=1.2$. Calculate the thickness of the film.

(Wisconsin)

Solution: Thus

Intensity maxima occur when the optical path difference is $\Delta=m \lambda$.

$$
\delta \Delta=\lambda \delta m .
$$

When the film is inserted as shown in Fig. 2.2, the optical path changes by

$$
\delta \Delta=t(n-1),
$$

where $t$ is the thickness of the film. As the interference pattern shifts by 4 fringes,

$$
\delta m=4 .
$$

Hence $t(n-1)=4 \lambda$, giving

$$
t=\frac{4 \lambda}{n-1}=10 \mu \mathrm{m} .
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-067.jpg?height=338&width=413&top_left_y=1012&top_left_x=492)

\section{4}

Consider the interference pattern from the three slits illustrated (Fig. 2.3). Assume the openings of the individual slits are the same $\left(\$ \frac{\lambda}{2}\right)$.

(a) At what value of $\theta$ is the first principal maximum? (i.e., the wavelets from all three slits are in phase).

(b) The result for (a) can be called $\theta_{1}$. The flux in the direction of seroth order maximum $(\theta=0)$ is $F_{0}$. What is the flux (in units of $F_{0}$ ) in the direction $\frac{\theta_{1}}{2}$ ? (Assume $\lambda \ll d_{\text {. }}$ ) 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-068.jpg?height=337&width=402&top_left_y=208&top_left_x=573)

Fig. $2.3$

\section{Solution:}

(a) The electric field $E(\theta)$ on the screen is the sum of the fields produced by the slits individually:

$$
E(\theta)=E_{1}+E_{2}+E_{3}=A+A e^{i \delta}+A e^{i \frac{5}{2} \delta},
$$

where

$$
\delta=\frac{2 \pi d}{\lambda} \sin \theta .
$$

Then the total intensity at $\theta$ is

$$
I(\theta) \sim E(\theta) \cdot E^{*}(\theta)=A^{2}\left\{3+2\left[\cos \delta+\cos \left(\frac{3 \delta}{2}\right)+\cos \left(\frac{5 \delta}{2}\right)\right]\right\} .
$$

For $\theta=0$, we have

$$
I(0) \sim 9 A^{2} \text {. }
$$

The expression for $I(\theta)$ shows that the first principal maximum occurs at $\delta=4 \pi$, i.e.,

or

$$
\left(\frac{2 \pi d}{\lambda}\right) \sin \theta_{1}=4 \pi,
$$

$$
\theta_{1} \approx \sin \theta_{1} \sim \frac{2 \lambda}{d} .
$$

(b)

$$
I\left(\frac{\theta_{1}}{2}\right) \sim A^{2}[3+2(\cos 2 \pi+\cos 3 \pi+\cos 5 \pi)]=A^{2} \sim \frac{I(0)}{9} .
$$
