\section{9}

A point source, $Q$, emits coherent light isotropically at two frequencies, $\omega$ and $\omega+\Delta \omega$, with equal power, $I$ joules/sec, at each frequency. Two detectors, $A$ and $B$, each with a (small) sensitive area $S$ capable of responding to individual photons, are located at distances $l_{A}$ and $l_{B}$ from $Q$ as shown in Fig. 3.12. In the following, take $\frac{\Delta \omega}{\omega} \ll 1$ and assume the experiment is carried out in vacuum.

(a) Calculate the individual photon counting rates (potons/sec) at A and at $B$ as functions of time. Consider time scales $>1 / \omega$.

(b) If now the output pulses from $A$ to $B$ are put into a coincidence circuit of resolving time $\tau$, what is the time-averaged coincidence counting rate? Assume that $r \ll 1 / \Delta \omega$ and recall that a coincidence circuit will produce an output pulse if the two input pulses arrive within a time $\tau$ of one another. 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-177.jpg?height=421&width=536&top_left_y=216&top_left_x=477)

Fig. 3.12

\section{Solution:}

(a) The waves of angular frequencies $\omega_{1}$ and $\omega_{2}$ arriving at $A$ can be represented by

$$
\begin{aligned}
E_{1} &=\frac{E_{0}}{l_{A}} e^{i\left(\omega_{1} t-k_{2} l_{A}\right)} \\
E_{2} &=\frac{E_{0}}{l_{A}} e^{i\left(\omega_{2} t-k_{2} l_{A}\right)}
\end{aligned}
$$

respectively, where $\omega_{1}=\omega, \omega_{2}=\omega+\Delta \omega$. The resultant wave at $A$ is then

$$
E_{\mathrm{A}}=E_{1}+E_{2}=\left(E_{0} / l_{\mathrm{A}}\right)\left[e^{i\left(\omega_{1} t-k_{1} l_{\mathrm{A}}\right)}+e^{i\left(\omega_{2} t-k_{2} l_{A}\right)}\right]
$$

and the intensity at $A$, time-averaged, is

$$
I_{\mathrm{A}} \sim \frac{1}{2} E_{\mathrm{A}} E_{\mathrm{A}}^{*}=\left(\frac{E_{0}}{l_{\mathrm{A}}}\right)^{2}\left\{1+\cos \left[\left(\omega_{2}-\omega_{1}\right) t-\left(k_{2}-k_{1}\right) l_{\mathrm{A}}\right]\right\} .
$$

The constant phase difference $\left(k_{2}-k_{1}\right) l_{A}$ can be taken as zero by choosing a suitable time origin. The intensity of the $\omega_{1}$ wave at $A$ is

As the source has power $I$ and is isotropic,

$$
I_{1} \sim \frac{1}{2} E_{1} E_{1}^{*}=\frac{1}{2}\left(\frac{E_{0}}{l_{\mathrm{A}}}\right)^{2} .
$$

giving

$$
I_{1}=\frac{I}{4 \pi l_{A}^{2}},
$$

Hence

$$
\left(\frac{E_{0}}{l_{A}}\right)^{2} \sim \frac{I}{2 \pi l_{A}^{2}} .
$$

The photon counting rate at $A$ is

$$
I_{A}=\frac{I}{2 \pi l_{A}^{2}}[1+\cos (\Delta \omega t)] .
$$

Similarly, we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
R_{\mathrm{A}}(t) &=\frac{I_{\mathrm{A}}(t) S}{\hbar \omega} . \\
&=\frac{I S}{2 \pi \hbar \omega l_{\mathrm{A}}^{2}}[1+\cos (\Delta \omega t)] .
\end{aligned}
$$

As

$$
I_{\mathrm{B}}(t)=\frac{I}{2 \pi l_{\mathrm{B}}^{2}}\left\{1+\cos \left[\Delta \omega t+\left(l_{\mathrm{B}}-l_{\mathrm{A}}\right)\left(k_{1}-k_{2}\right) \mid\right\} .\right.
$$

$$
\begin{aligned}
&k_{1}=\frac{\omega_{1}}{c}=\frac{\omega}{c}, \\
&k_{2}=\frac{\omega_{2}}{c}=\frac{\omega+\Delta \omega}{c}, \\
&k_{1}-k_{2}=-\frac{\Delta \omega}{c},
\end{aligned}
$$

we have

$$
I_{B}(t)=\frac{I}{2 \pi l_{B}^{2}}\left\{1+\cos \left[\Delta \omega t+\frac{\left(l_{A}-l_{B}\right)}{c} \Delta \omega\right]\right\},
$$

giving

$$
R_{\mathrm{B}}(t)=\frac{I S}{2 \pi \hbar \omega l_{\mathrm{A}}^{2}}\left\{1+\cos \left[\Delta \omega t+\frac{\left(l_{\mathrm{A}}-l_{\mathrm{B}}\right) \Delta \omega}{c}\right]\right\} .
$$

(b) The probability that a signal from $A$ at time $t$ be accompanied by one from $B$ within a time $\pm \tau$ is $2 \tau R_{\mathrm{B}}(t)$. Hence the coincidence counting rate is

$$
\begin{aligned}
R_{\mathrm{AB}}(t) &=R_{\mathrm{A}}(t) R_{\mathrm{B}}(t)(2 \tau) \\
&=\frac{I^{2} S^{2} \tau}{2 \pi^{2} \hbar^{2} \omega^{2} l_{\mathrm{A}}^{2} l_{\mathrm{B}}^{2}}[1+\cos (\Delta \omega t)]\left\{1+\cos \left[\Delta \omega\left(t+\frac{l_{\mathrm{A}}-l_{\mathrm{B}}}{c}\right)\right]\right\} .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{0}

Describe briefly how a photomultiplier tube works. Can such a tube be used to distinguish between two photons whose energies differ by $50 \%$ ?

(Columbia) 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-179.jpg?height=243&width=976&top_left_y=247&top_left_x=227)

Fig. $3.13$

\section{Solution:}

A photomultiplier tube is a device which multiplies a single photoelectron by five or six orders of magnitude. In addition to a photo-emission cathode, it has several dynodes which are maintained at successively higher potentials (see Fig. 3.13). Generally the potential is increased by 100 -volt steps from one dynode to the next. The potential difference accelerates the electrons so that when they strike the surface of a dynode, each causes three or four secondary electrons to be ejected. These secondary electrons are then accelerated towards the next dynode and the process is repeated. In this manner, a single electron at the cathode can be multiplied to one million electrons at the anode after ten stages.

The resultant anode current $I$, which is proportional to the mean number $\bar{N}$ of the electrons collected, is on average proportional to the energy $E$ of the incoming photon. The number $N$ of the electrons collected satisfies the Poisson distribution

$$
\mathrm{P}(N)=\bar{N}^{N} e^{-N} / N !
$$

For large $\bar{N}$, the Poisson distribution approximates the Gaussian distribution:

$$
\mathrm{P}(N) \approx \frac{1}{\sqrt{2 \pi} \sigma} \exp \left[-\frac{(N-\bar{N})^{2}}{2 \sigma^{2}}\right], \sigma=\sqrt{\bar{N}} .
$$

The half-width of the distribution, $\Delta N$, (see Fig. 3.14) is given by

i.e.,

$$
\mathrm{P}\left(\bar{N}+\frac{\Delta N}{2}\right) / \mathrm{P}(\bar{N})=\frac{1}{2}=\exp \left[-\frac{(\Delta N / 2)^{2}}{2 \sigma^{2}}\right]
$$

$$
\Delta N=2 \sqrt{2 \ln 2 \cdot \sigma^{2}}=2 \sqrt{2 \bar{N} \ln 2} .
$$

For a tube with 10 dynodes, each of secondary-emission rate $5, \bar{N} \simeq$ $5^{10}$. From Fig. 3.15, we see that to distinguish two photons of energies $E_{1}$ and $E_{2}$ differing by $50 \%$ we require

$$
\frac{\Delta N}{\bar{N}} \approx 2 \sqrt{\frac{2 \ln 2}{5^{10}}} \leq 50 \% .
$$

As this is satisfied, we conclude that such a tube can distinguish between the two photons.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-180.jpg?height=342&width=490&top_left_y=614&top_left_x=192)

Fig. $3.14$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-180.jpg?height=375&width=645&top_left_y=577&top_left_x=691)

Fig. 3.15

\section{1}

(a) What is meant by

(i) the Doppler line width, and

(ii) the natural line width of a spectral line?

(b) Describe an experiment for making "Doppler free" measurements of spectral lines. (You need not be quantitative, but your answer should make clear that you understand the basic physics involved.)

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) (i) The Doppler broadening of spectral lines is caused by the random thermal motion of the radiating atoms and is proportional to $T^{1 / 2}$, where $T$ is the absolute temperature of the source.

(ii) According to Heisenberg's uncertainty principal, $\Delta t \cdot \Delta E \sim h$, an excited state of lifetime $\Delta t$ of an atom has an uncertainty in energy of $\Delta E$. The corresponding spectral line $\nu=\frac{E}{h}$ therefore has a natural width $\Delta \nu=\frac{\Delta E}{h} \sim \frac{1}{\Delta t}$. (b) The method of double-photon absorption can be employed to make "Doppler free" measurements of spectral lines. A molecule, having absorbed two photons $\hbar \omega_{1}\left(k_{1}\right)$ and $\hbar \omega_{2}\left(\mathbf{k}_{2}\right)$, would be exited from state $i$ to state $f$. If the molecule is motionless,

$$
E_{\mathrm{f}}-E_{\mathrm{i}}=\hbar\left(\omega_{1}+\omega_{2}\right),
$$

and no Doppler shift occurs. If the molecule moves with velocity $\mathbf{v}$,

$$
E_{\mathrm{f}}-E_{\mathrm{i}}=\hbar\left[\omega_{1}+\omega_{2}-\mathrm{v} \cdot\left(\mathrm{k}_{1}+\mathbf{k}_{2}\right)\right],
$$

and a Doppler shift $\mathbf{v} \cdot\left(\mathbf{k}_{1}+\mathbf{k}_{2}\right)$ occurs. The Doppler shift is sero if $\mathbf{k}_{1}=-\mathbf{k}_{2}$.

Two beams of laser light of the same frequency $\omega$ are incident in op posite directions on a gas sample as shown in Fig. 3.16. Double-photon absorption that takes place can be detected by the $f \rightarrow m$ (an intermediate state) fluorescent transition.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-181.jpg?height=385&width=834&top_left_y=908&top_left_x=292)

Fig. 3.16

\section{2}

(a) Consider the emission or absorption of visible light by the molecules of a hot gas. Derive an expression for the frequency distribution $f(\nu)$ expected for a spectral line, central frequency $\nu_{0}$, due to the Doppler broadening. Assume an ideal gas at temperature $T$ with molecular weight $M$.

Consider a vessel filled with argon gas at a pressure of 10 Torr(1 Torr $=$ $1 \mathrm{~mm}$ of mercury) and a temperature of $200^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$. Inside the vessel is a small piece of sodium which is heated so that the vessel will contain some sodium vapor. We observe the sodium absorption line at $5896 \AA \AA$ in the light from a tungsten filament passing through the vessel. Estimate: 
(b) The magnitude of the Doppler broadening of the line.
(c) The magnitude of the collision broadening of the line.

Assume here that the number of sodium atoms is very small compared to the number of argon atoms. Make reasonable estimates of quantities that you may need which are not given and express your answers for the broadening in Angstroms.

\section{Atomic weight of sodium $=23$.}

(CUSPEA)

\section{Solution:} by

(a) The Doppler frequency shift, viewed along the $z$ direction, is given

i.e.,

$$
\nu=\nu_{0}\left(1+\frac{v_{s}}{c}\right)
$$

$$
v_{s}=c\left(\frac{\nu-\nu_{0}}{\nu_{0}}\right) \text {. }
$$

The velocities of the molecules follow the Maxwell-Boltsmann distribution

$$
d P=\left(\frac{M}{2 \pi R T}\right)^{3 / 2} e^{-\frac{M v^{2}}{2 \pi T}} d v_{x} d v_{y} d v_{x},
$$

where $M$ is the molecular weight of the gas and $R$ is the gas constant. Integrating over $v_{x}$ and $v_{y}$ from sero to infinity yields the distribution for $v_{x}$ :

$$
d P=\left(\frac{M}{2 \pi R T}\right)^{1 / 2} e^{-\frac{M v^{2}}{2 R 2}} d v_{x}
$$

giving the frequency distribution

$$
d P=\frac{1}{\nu_{0}}\left(\frac{M c^{2}}{2 \pi R T}\right)^{1 / 2} e^{-M c^{2}\left(\nu-\nu_{0}\right)^{2} / 2 R T \nu_{0}^{2}} d \nu .
$$

(b) The frequency distribution has the form of a Gaussian distribution with the standard deviation $\sigma$ give by

Hence

$$
\sigma^{2}=\frac{R T \nu_{0}^{2}}{M c^{2}} .
$$

$$
\Delta \nu \approx \sqrt{\frac{R T}{M c^{2}}} \nu_{0},
$$

or

$$
\begin{aligned}
\Delta \lambda & \approx \sqrt{\frac{R T}{M c^{2}}} \lambda_{0}=\sqrt{\frac{(8.3)(473)}{\left(40 \times 10^{-3}\right)}} \frac{\lambda_{0}}{\left(3 \times 10^{8}\right)} \\
&=1.04 \times 10^{-6} \lambda_{0}=1.04 \times 10^{-6} \times 5896 \AA=6.13 \times 10^{-3} \mathrm{~A} .
\end{aligned}
$$

(c) The mean free path $\Lambda$ is given by

$$
\Lambda=\frac{1}{n \sigma},
$$

where $\sigma=\pi r^{2} \approx 3 \times 10^{-20} \mathrm{~m}^{2}$, taking $r \approx 10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}$. The number of molecules per unit volume is

$$
n=\frac{A}{V}=\frac{p A}{R T},
$$

where $A$ is Avogadro's number. Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
n &=\frac{\left(1.01 \times 10^{5} \times 10 / 760\right)}{(8.3)(473)} \times 6.02 \times 10^{23} \\
&=2.04 \times 10^{23} \mathrm{~m}^{-3} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Substituting gives $\Lambda=1.7 \times 10^{-4} \mathrm{~m}$. The average time interval between two collisions is $\tau=\Lambda / v$, where the $v$ is the mean speed of a sodium atom,

Thus

$$
v \approx \sqrt{\frac{R T}{M}}=\sqrt{\frac{(8.3)(473)}{23 \times 10^{-3}}} \approx 413 \mathrm{~ms}^{-1} \text {. }
$$

$$
\tau=\frac{1.7 \times 10^{-4}}{413}=4 \times 10^{-7} \mathrm{~s}
$$

The broadening due to collision is given by the collision frequency $\Delta \nu=$ 1/T. In terms of wavelength, the broadening is

$$
\Delta \lambda=\frac{c}{\nu^{2}} \Delta \nu=\frac{\lambda^{2}}{c \tau}=3 \times 10^{-5} \stackrel{\circ}{\mathrm{A}}
$$



\section{8}

Consider a gas of atoms $A$ at $T=300 \mathrm{~K}, p=100$ Torr. The mass of each atom is $4.2 \times 10^{-27} \mathrm{~kg}$ and Boltzmann's constant is $1.4 \times 10^{-23} \mathrm{~J} / \mathrm{K}$. Some of the atoms are in excited states $A^{*}$ and emit radiation of frequency $\nu$.

(a) Estimate the Doppler width $\Delta \nu_{\mathrm{D}} / \nu$.

(b) Assume a reasonable cross-section for $A^{*}$-A collision and estimate the pressure-broadened width of the line $\Delta \nu_{p} / \nu$.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a)

$$
\frac{\Delta \nu_{\mathrm{D}}}{\nu}=\frac{1}{c} \sqrt{\frac{k T}{m}}=\frac{1}{3 \times 10^{8}} \sqrt{\frac{1.4 \times 10^{-23} \times 300}{4.2 \times 10^{-27}}}=3 \times 10^{-6} .
$$

(b) The pressure-broadened width $\Delta \nu_{p}$ is the collision frequency of a molecule of the gas

$$
\Delta \nu_{p}=v / \Lambda,
$$

where $v$ is its mean speed, given by $\sqrt{k T / m}$, and $\Lambda$ is the mean free path of a molecule, given by

$$
\frac{1}{\pi r^{2} n}=\frac{1}{\pi r^{2}} \sqrt{\frac{k T}{p}} \text {, }
$$

$r$ being the radius of a molecule $\approx 10^{-10} \mathrm{~m}, n$ the number of molecules per unit volume. Thus

$$
\Delta \nu_{p}=\pi r^{2} n \sqrt{\frac{k T}{m}}=\frac{\pi p r^{2}}{\sqrt{k m T}}=7.2 \times 10^{7} \mathrm{~Hz} .
$$

For visible light, $\lambda \approx 5000 \AA$ or $\nu=6 \times 10^{14} \mathrm{~Hz}$,

$$
\Delta \nu_{p} / \nu=1.2 \times 10^{-7} \text {. }
$$

\section{4}

An electronic transition in ions of ${ }^{12} \mathrm{C}$ leads to photon emission near $\lambda=500 \mathrm{~nm}(h \nu=2.5 \mathrm{eV})$. The ions are in thermal equilibrium at a temperature $k T=20 \mathrm{eV}$, a density $n=10^{24} / \mathrm{m}^{3}$, and a non-uniform magnetic field which ranges up to $B=1$ Tesla. (a) Briefly discuss broadening mechanisms which might cause the transition to have an observed width $\Delta \lambda$ greater than that obtained for very small values of $T, n$ and $B$.

(b) For one of these mechanisms calculate the broadened width $\Delta \lambda$ using order of magnitude estimates of the needed parameters.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Thermal motion of ions broadens spectral lines because of the Doppler effect. The broadening is proportional to $\sqrt{T}$, where $T$ is the absolute temperature.

Collisions between ions also cause a broadening, the collision or preasure broadening, which is proportional to $\sqrt{T}$ and to $n$, the number density of ions, or $p$, the pressure of the gas.

When a source is placed in a magnetic field, a spectral line may break up with the energy shifts proportional to $B$, the strength of the magnetic field. This spliting is known as the Zeeman effect. If the magnetic field is non-uniform, the spectral shifts would range from zero to a maximum value dependent on $B$, i.e., the spectral lines are broadened with the width proportional to $B$.

(b) The Doppler broadening is given by

Thus

$$
\begin{aligned}
\frac{\Delta \lambda}{\lambda} &=\frac{1}{c} \sqrt{\frac{k T}{m}}=\frac{1}{3 \times 10^{8}}\left(\frac{20 \times 1.6 \times 10^{-19}}{12 \times 1.67 \times 10^{-27}}\right)^{1 / 2} \\
&=\frac{1.3 \times 10^{4}}{3 \times 10^{8}} \approx 4 \times 10^{-5} .
\end{aligned}
$$

$$
\Delta \lambda=5000 \times 4 \times 10^{-5}=0.2 \AA \text {. }
$$

The collision broadening is given by

or

$$
\Delta \nu \approx r^{2} n \sqrt{\frac{k T}{m}}=10^{-20} \times 10^{24} \times 1.3 \times 10^{4}=1.3 \times 10^{8} \mathrm{~s}^{-1},
$$

$$
\Delta \lambda=\lambda^{2} \Delta \nu / c \approx 0.1 \AA \text {. }
$$

The energy shift of a spectral line caused by the Zeeman effect is given by

$$
\Delta E=\mu_{\mathrm{B}} B \Delta(M g),
$$

where $M$ is the magnetic quantum number, $g$ the Land√© factor, $\mu_{\mathrm{B}}$ the Bohr magneton

$$
\mu_{\mathrm{B}}=\frac{c h}{4 \pi m c}=0.93 \times 10^{-23} \mathrm{~A} \cdot \mathrm{m}^{-2} \text {. }
$$

Taking $\Delta(M g) \sim 1$, we have, with $B=1$ Tesla,

$$
\Delta E \approx 10^{-23} \mathrm{~J} \text {. }
$$

\section{Hence}

$$
\Delta \lambda=\frac{\lambda^{2} \Delta \nu}{c}=\frac{\lambda^{2} \Delta E}{h c} \approx\left(\frac{5000^{2} \times 10^{-23}}{6 \times 10^{-34} \times 3 \times 10^{18}}\right)=0.1 \AA .
$$

\section{5}

During the days when there are no clouds and the atmosphere is relatively dust (smog) free and has low humidity, the sky, away from the sun, has a very deep blue color. This is due to molecular scattering.

(a) If the molecular electric polarizability due to the photon $\mathbf{E}$ field is frequency independent, show how this leads to a wavelength dependence of the scattering of sunlight similar to that observed. Discuss the angular dependence of the scattering.

(b) If in each air volume element $\Delta V$ of sides small compared with $\lambda$, the mean number of molecules is $N_{0} \Delta V \gg 1$, and if there is no fluctuation about this mean number, show that no net scattering results.

(c) If the root-mean-square fluctuation of the number of molecules equals the square root of the mean number, show that the net scattering intensity is as if each molecule scattered independently with no interference between molecules.

(d) When the relative humidity is near $100 \%$ there is observed to be enhanced scattering (relative to (c)) for water molecules. Explain.

(Columbia)

\section{Solution:}

(a) The electric field $\mathbf{E}=\mathbf{E}_{0} \cos (\omega t)$ of the incident light polarizes the air molecules into oscillating dipoles, each of moment $\mathbf{p}_{0} \cos (\omega t)$, where

$$
\mathbf{p}_{0}=\frac{e^{2}}{m} \frac{\mathbf{E}_{0}}{\left(\omega_{0}^{2}-\omega^{2}\right)},
$$

$e$ and $m$ being the charge and mass of an electron, $\omega_{0}$ its characteristic frequency. For optical frequencies, $\omega \ll \omega_{0}$, so that the polarizability $e^{2} / m \omega_{0}^{2}$ is independent of frequency.

The field of the radiation scattered by such an induced dipole has an amplitude at a large distance $r$ from the dipole of

$$
E=\frac{1}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0}}\left(\frac{\omega}{c}\right)^{2} \frac{\sin \theta}{r} p_{0},
$$

where $\theta$ is the angle between $\mathbf{r}$ and the orientation of the dipole. The corresponding scattered intensity is

$$
\begin{aligned}
I &=\frac{1}{2} E H=\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{\epsilon_{0}}{\mu_{0}}} E^{2}=\frac{\omega^{4}}{32 \pi^{2} \epsilon_{0} c^{3}}\left(\frac{\sin \theta}{r}\right)^{2} p_{0}^{2} \\
&=\left(\frac{e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} m c^{2}}\right)^{2} \frac{\epsilon_{0} c E_{0}^{2}}{2}\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega_{0}}\right)^{4}\left(\frac{\sin \theta}{r}\right)^{2} \\
&=a^{2} I_{0}\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega_{0}}\right)^{4}\left(\frac{\sin \theta}{r}\right)^{2},
\end{aligned}
$$

where $a$ is the classical radius of an electron and $I_{0}$ the intensity of the incident light. Thus $I \propto \omega^{4} \propto \frac{1}{\lambda^{4}}$. Hence, of the visible light the blue color is preferentially scattered. This accounts for the blueness of the sky if we are not watching along the line of sight of the sun.

The scattered intensity has an angular distribution $\sim \sin ^{2} \theta$. For an unpolarised incident light, let its direction be along the $z$-axis and consider light scattered into a direction in the $x z$ plane making angle $\psi$ with the $z$-axis as shown in Fig. 3.17(a). At some instant, the electric field of the incident light at the dipole, $\mathbf{E}_{0}$, (and hence the induced dipole) makes angle $\phi$ with the $x$-axis. Geometry gives

$$
\cos \theta=\cos \phi \sin \psi .
$$

As $\phi$ is random, we ahave to average $\sin ^{2} \theta$ over $\phi:$

$$
\left\langle\sin ^{2} \theta\right\rangle=\frac{1}{2 \pi} \int_{0}^{2 \pi}\left(1-\cos ^{2} \phi \sin ^{2} \psi\right) d \phi=\frac{1}{2}\left(1+\cos ^{2} \psi\right) .
$$

Hence the scattered intensity for an unpolarised incident light has an angular distribution $\sim\left(1+\cos ^{2} \psi\right)$. The scattered intensity is maximum in the forward and backward directions, and is minimum in a transverse direction. (b) Consider a small volume element $\Delta V \& \lambda^{3} \ll r^{3}$. Imagine it as being made up of $q$ equal layers, each of thickness $<\lambda, \sqrt{2} \lambda / q$ apart and making an angle $\frac{\pi}{4}$ with the incident light as shown in Fig. 3.17(b). The phase difference between any two adjacent layers is

$$
\Delta \delta=\frac{2 \pi}{\lambda} \frac{\sqrt{2} \lambda}{q} \sin \frac{\pi}{4}=\frac{2 \pi}{q} .
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-188.jpg?height=474&width=615&top_left_y=486&top_left_x=133)

(a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-188.jpg?height=526&width=589&top_left_y=468&top_left_x=742)

(b)

Fig. 3.17

Let the field of the light scattered in the direction $\psi$ by the $k$-th layer be $E_{k} \cos \left(\omega t-\delta_{k}\right)$. The resultant field in that direction is then

$$
E=\sum_{k=1}^{q} E_{k} \cos \left(\omega t-\delta_{k}\right)
$$

with

$$
\delta_{k}-\delta_{k-1}=\frac{2 \pi}{q} \text {. }
$$

If the molecules are uniformly distributed in the volume element with no fuctuation, then $E_{1}=E_{2}=\ldots=E_{q}$. Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
E &=E_{1} \sum_{k=0}^{q-1} \cos \left(\omega t-\delta_{1}+\frac{2 k \pi}{q}\right) \\
&=E_{1} \operatorname{Re}\left\{e^{i\left(\omega t-\delta_{1}\right)} \sum_{k=0}^{q-1} e^{i \frac{2 k \pi}{q}}\right\} \\
&=E_{1} \operatorname{Re}\left\{e^{i\left(\omega t-\delta_{1}\right)}\left(\frac{1-e^{i 2 \pi}}{1-e^{i \frac{2 \pi}{q}}}\right)\right\}=0 .
\end{aligned}
$$

Therefore, no net scattering results.

(c) If the number $N$ of molecules in each layer fluctuates about a mean $\bar{N}$, the $E_{k}$ 's also fluctuate about a mean $\bar{E}$ :

$$
E_{k}=\bar{E}+\Delta_{k} .
$$

Then

so that

$$
\begin{aligned}
E &=\sum_{k=1}^{q}\left(\bar{E}+\Delta_{k}\right) \cos \left(\omega t-\delta_{k}\right) \\
&=\sum_{k=1}^{q} \Delta_{k} \cos \left(\omega t-\delta_{k}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

$$
\begin{aligned}
E^{2}=& \sum_{k=1}^{q} \Delta_{k}^{2} \cos ^{2}\left(\omega t-\delta_{k}\right) \\
&+\sum_{k=1}^{q} \sum_{l \neq k} \Delta_{k} \Delta_{l} \cos \left(\omega t-\delta_{k}\right) \cos \left(\omega t-\delta_{l}\right) \\
\approx & \sum_{k=1}^{q} \Delta_{k}^{2} \cos \left(\omega t-\delta_{k}\right)
\end{aligned}
$$

since the $\Delta_{k}$ 's are independent of one another and are as likely positive as negative.

We can write

$$
E^{2} \approx q\left\langle\Delta_{k}^{2}\right) \cos ^{2}(\omega t-\delta) .
$$

From Eq. (1) we see that

Then

$$
\Delta_{k}^{2}=\left(\frac{1}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0}}\right)^{2}\left(\frac{\omega}{c}\right)^{4}\left(\frac{\sin \theta}{r}\right)^{2}\left(N_{k}-\bar{N}\right)^{2} p_{0}^{2} .
$$

$$
\left\langle\Delta_{k}^{2}\right\rangle=\left(\frac{1}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0}}\right)^{2}\left(\frac{\omega}{c}\right)^{4}\left(\frac{\sin \theta}{r}\right)^{2} \bar{N} p_{0}^{2}
$$

as it is given that $\left\langle\left(N_{k}-\bar{N}\right)^{2}\right\rangle=\bar{N}$.

Hence

$$
\begin{aligned}
I &=\frac{1}{2} \sqrt{\frac{\epsilon_{0}}{\mu_{0}}} q\left\langle\Delta_{k}^{2}\right\rangle \\
&=\frac{\omega^{4}}{32 \pi^{2} \epsilon_{0} c^{3}}\left(\frac{\sin \theta}{r}\right)^{2} q \bar{N} p_{0}^{2} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Now $q \bar{N}$ is the total number of molecules in $\Delta V$. A comparison of the above with Eq. (2) shows that the net scattered intensity is the same as if each of the molecules scattered independently with no interference between them.

For a dilute gas of volume $V$ and $N_{0}$ moleclules per unit volume, we then have from Eq. (2)

$$
I=\left(\frac{e^{2}}{4 \pi \epsilon_{0} m c^{2}}\right) I_{0}\left(\frac{\omega}{\omega_{0}}\right)^{4}\left(\frac{\sin \theta}{r}\right)^{2} N_{0} V .
$$

If its refractive index is $n$, the Clausius-Mossotti relation

$$
n^{2}-1=\frac{N_{0} e^{2}}{\epsilon_{0} m \omega_{0}^{2}}
$$

gives

$$
I=\pi^{2} I_{0} \frac{\left(n^{2}-1\right)^{2}}{\lambda^{4}} \frac{V}{N}\left(\frac{\sin \theta}{r}\right)^{2},
$$

which is known as Rayleigh's formula.

(d) When the relative humidity is near $100 \%$, water molecules condense to form droplets. These fine particles scatter the blue color when a beam of white light passes through them. This is known as Tyndall scattering and is much stronger than Rayleigh scattering.

A typical molecular gas shows absorption bands over the entire electromagnetic spectrum from $\mathrm{X}$-rays to radio waves. In terms of atomic and molecular structure state the absorption process responsible for absorption bands in the following regions of the spectrum:
(a) X-ray,
(b) ultraviolet and visible,
(c) near infra-red,
(d) far infra-red and radio.

(Wisconsin)

\section{Solution:}

When electromagnetic waves pass through a gas into a spectroscope, those wavelengths which the medium would emit if its temperature were raised high enough will be absorbed, so that the spectrum will contain dark lines on regions corresponding to upward transitions between the energy levels of the atoms or molecules of the gas.

(a) X-ray: Removal of an electron from the inner shell of an atom to an outer shell or out of the atom.

(b) Ultraviolet and visible: Transition between the energy states of an orbiting atomic electron.

molecule.

(c) Near infra-red: Transition between the vibrational levels of a molecule.

(d) Far infra-red: Transition between the rotational levels of a

\section{8}

Consider the earth's atmosphere (consisting of $\mathrm{O}_{2}, \mathrm{~N}_{2}, \mathrm{CO}_{2}, \mathrm{~N}_{2} \mathrm{O}, \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ $\mathrm{O}_{3}$ etc.). Discuss whether the atmosphere is reasonably transparent or strongly absorbing for each of the following frequency regions. If absorbing, say what are the most important mechanisms.
(a) $10^{8}-10^{\circ} \mathrm{Hs}$.
(b) far infra-red
(c) near infra-red
(d) visible
(e) ultra-violet
(f) X-rays
(g) $\gamma$-rays

(Columbia)

\section{Solution:}

(a) Microwaves of frequencies $10^{8}-10^{9} \mathrm{Hs}$ are strongly absorbed by $\mathrm{O}_{2}, \mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$ and $\mathrm{N}_{2} \mathrm{O}$.

(b) Far infra-red light is strongly absorbed by $\mathrm{CO}_{2}$.

(c) Near infra-red light is strongly absorbed by $\mathrm{H}_{2} \mathrm{O}$.

(e) Ultra-violet light is strongly absorbed by $\mathrm{O}_{3}$.

(d), (f), (g) The atmosphere is transparent to $X$-rays, $\gamma$-rays and the visible light, though some spectral lines of the last are absorbed by $\mathrm{O}_{3}$.

\section{9}

(a) the blue sky
(b) the red sun at sunset
(c) the rainbow
(e) the twinkling of stars.

\section{Solution:}

(a) The intensity of the radiation scattered by air molecules is, according to Rayleigh's scattering formula, inversely proportional to the fourth power of the wavelength. Hence, the shorter wavelength (blue color) component of the visible sun light is more strongly scattered and the sky appears blue when the observation is not along the line of illumination (see Fig. 3.18(a)).

(b) At sunset or sunrise, the sun light passes through a thicker layer of the atmosphere than at noon and its blue components are scattered out. Consequently the sun looks reddish (see Fig. 3.18(b)).

Fig. $3.18$ (c) A quantity of small water droplets remains in the atmosphere after rain which refract the sunlight. As the index of refraction of water is dependent on wavelength, components of sunlight of different wavelengths or colors are refracted into different angles, giving rise to the rainbow (see Fig. 3.19). of stars.

\section{1}

Sunlight is normally incident on the surface of water with index of refraction $n=1.33$.

(a) Derive the energy reflection and transmission coefficients, $R$ and $T \cdot(R+T=1)$

(b) If the incident fux is $1 \mathrm{~kW} / \mathrm{m}^{2}$, what is the pressure that sunlight exerts on the surface of the water?

Be careful: part (b) may not follow from part (a) as directly as you think! 

\section{Solution:}

(a) Fresnel's formulae give for normally incidence

$$
\begin{aligned}
&R=\left(\frac{n_{2}-n_{1}}{n_{2}+n_{1}}\right)^{2}=\left(\frac{n-1}{n+1}\right)^{2}=0.02 \\
&T=\frac{4 n_{1} n_{2}}{\left(n_{2}+n_{1}\right)^{2}}=\frac{4 n}{(n+1)^{2}}=0.98 \\
&R+T=1
\end{aligned}
$$

(b) From the point of view of the photon theory, light pressure is the result of a transfer of the momentum of the incoming light to the target. If $W$ is the incident flux density and $R$ the reflection coefficient, the change of momentum per unit area is

$$
\frac{W R}{c}-\left(-\frac{W}{c}\right)=\frac{(1+R) W}{c} .
$$

\section{By definition this is the light pressure $p$}

$$
p=\frac{(1+0.02) \times 1000}{3 \times 10^{8}}=3.34 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{~N} / \mathrm{m}^{2} .
$$

\section{2}

An electromagnetic wave $\left(\mathbf{E}_{0}, \mathbf{H}_{0}\right)$ traveling in a medium with index of refraction $n_{1}$ strikes a planar interface with a medium with $n_{2}$. The angle the wave makes with the normal to the interface is $\theta_{1}$. Derive the general Fresnel equations for the reflected and refracted waves. Obtain Brewster's law as a special case of these equations.

(Columbia)

\section{Solution:}

If the incident electromagnetic wave is unpolarized, it can be considered as consisting of two components, one plane polarized with the electric field in the plane of incidence, and the other plane polarized with the electric field perpendicular to the plane of incidence. Consider the first component and let the electric and magnetic vectors of the incident, reflected and refracted waves be represented by $E_{p_{1}}, E_{p_{1}}^{\prime}, E_{p_{2}}$ and $H_{s_{1}}, H_{s_{1}}^{\prime}, H_{s_{2}}$, respectively (See Fig. 3.20). The boundary conditions on the field vectors give

$$
\begin{aligned}
&E_{p_{1}} \cos \theta_{1}+E_{p_{1}}^{\prime} \cos \theta_{1}=E_{p_{2}} \cos \theta_{2}, \\
&H_{p_{1}}-H_{\theta_{1}}^{\prime}=H_{s_{2}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-195.jpg?height=342&width=369&top_left_y=552&top_left_x=508)

Fig. 2.20

\section{For a plane electromagnetic wave,}

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sqrt{\epsilon_{1}} E_{p_{1}} &=\sqrt{\mu_{1}} H_{s_{1}}, \\
\sqrt{\epsilon_{1}} E_{p_{1}}^{\prime} &=\sqrt{\mu_{1}} H_{s_{1}}^{\prime}, \\
\sqrt{\epsilon_{2}} E_{p_{2}} &=\sqrt{\mu_{2}} H_{s_{2}} .
\end{aligned}
$$

\section{Assuming the media to be non-ferromagnetic, we have}

$$
\mu_{1}=\mu_{2}=\mu_{0},
$$

and so

$$
n_{1}=\sqrt{\frac{\epsilon_{1}}{\epsilon_{0}}}, n_{2}=\sqrt{\frac{\epsilon_{2}}{\epsilon_{0}}} .
$$

\section{Equation (2) can then be written as}

$$
n_{1} E_{p_{1}}-n_{1} E_{p_{1}}^{\prime}=n_{2} E_{p_{2}} .
$$

Snell's law gives $n_{1} \sin \theta_{1}=n_{2} \sin \theta_{2}$. Combining this with Eqs. (1) and (2) yields

$$
r_{p} \equiv \frac{E_{p_{1}}^{\prime}}{E_{p_{1}}^{\prime}}=\frac{\tan \left(\theta_{2}-\theta_{1}\right)}{\tan \left(\theta_{2}+\theta_{1}\right)}, t_{p} \equiv \frac{E_{p_{2}}}{E_{p_{1}}}=\frac{2 \sin \theta_{2} \cos \theta_{1}}{\sin \left(\theta_{2}+\theta_{1}\right) \cos \left(\theta_{2}-\theta_{1}\right)},
$$

where $r_{p}$ and $t_{p}$ are called the amplitude reflection and transmission coefficients respectively.

Similarly, for the incident component with its electric field vector perpendicular to the plane of incidence we have

$$
\begin{aligned}
r_{8} & \equiv \frac{E_{s_{1}}^{\prime}}{E_{s_{1}}}=\frac{\sin \left(\theta_{2}-\theta_{1}\right)}{\sin \left(\theta_{2}+\theta_{1}\right)}, \\
t_{3} \equiv \frac{E_{\theta_{2}}}{E_{s_{1}}}=\frac{2 \sin \theta_{2} \cos \theta_{1}}{\sin \left(\theta_{2}+\theta_{1}\right)} .
\end{aligned}
$$

Note that $r_{p}=0$ when $\theta_{1}+\theta_{2}=\frac{\pi}{2}$, i.e., when

$$
n_{1} \sin \theta_{1}=n_{2} \cos \theta_{2},
$$

or

$$
\theta_{1}=\tan ^{-1}\left(\frac{n_{2}}{n_{1}}\right) .
$$

This is stated as Brewster's law: When the incident angle is equal to $\tan ^{-1}\left(\theta_{2} / \theta_{1}\right)$, the component with $\mathbf{E}$ in the plane of incidence is not reflected and an unpolarized wave becomes plane polarized on reflection.

\section{8}

Calculate the ratio of the intensities of the reflected wave and the incident wave for a light wave incident normally on the surface of a deep body of water with index of refraction $n=1.33$.

(Wisconsin)

Solution:

Fresnel's equations give for light incident normally

$$
R=\frac{I_{\mathrm{r}}}{I_{\mathrm{i}}}=\left(\frac{n_{2}-n_{1}}{n_{2}+n_{1}}\right)^{2}=0.02 .
$$

3024

While an aquarium is being filled with water (index of refraction $n>$ 1), a motionless fish looks up vertically through the rising surface of the water at a stationary monochromatic plane wave light source. Does the fish see the light source blue-shifted (higher frequency), red-shifted (lower frequency), or is the frequency change identically sero? Explain your reasoning.

(Columbia)

\section{Solution:}

The speed of light propagating through a moving medium of index of refraction $n$ with speed $V$ relative to an observer was given by Fresnel as

$$
u=\frac{c}{n}-V\left(1-\frac{1}{n^{2}}\right),
$$

which has been experimentally verified by Fiseau. The wavelength of light propagating through water is

$$
\lambda_{\text {water }}=\frac{c}{n \nu},
$$

where $c$ and $\nu$ are the speed and frequency of light in vacuum. The frequency of light as seen by the fish is

$$
\nu^{\prime}=\frac{u}{\lambda_{\text {water }}}=\nu-V\left(1-\frac{1}{n^{2}}\right)\left(\frac{n \nu}{c}\right) .
$$

For $n>1, \nu^{\prime}<\nu$, the fish would see the light red-shifted.

\section{5}

Solar energy at the rate $800 \mathrm{~W} / \mathrm{m}^{2}$ strikes a flat solar panel for water heating. If the panel has absorptance $=0.96$ for all wavelengths and the sides are perfect insulators, calculate the maximum temperature of the water. If the absorptance dropped by $1 / 2$, how would this affect the final temperature?

(Wisconsin) 

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-198.jpg?height=388&width=403&top_left_y=246&top_left_x=575)

Fig. $3.21$

\section{Solution:}

The Stefan-Boltzmann law gives, under equilibrium,

$$
\alpha \phi=\sigma T^{4}
$$

where $\sigma=5.67 \times 10^{-8} \mathrm{~W} / \mathrm{m}^{2} \mathrm{~K}^{4}$ is Stefan's constant, $\phi$ is the solar energy flux density, and $\alpha$ is the absorptance of the panel. With

$$
\begin{aligned}
\sigma &=5.67 \times 10^{-8} \mathrm{~W} / \mathrm{m}^{2} \mathrm{~K}^{4}, \\
\alpha &=0.96, \\
\phi &=800 \mathrm{~W} / \mathrm{m}^{2},
\end{aligned}
$$

we obtain $T=341 \mathrm{~K}$ for the maximum temperature of the water. As

$$
T \propto \alpha^{1 / 4},
$$

when $\alpha^{\prime}=\alpha / 2$, we have

$$
T^{\prime}=(1 / 2)^{1 / 4} \mathrm{~T}=286 \mathrm{~K} .
$$

Thus if the absorptance dropped by $1 / 2$, the maximum temperature would be $286 \mathrm{~K}$.

\section{6}

Estimate the intensity of moonlight on earth.

(a) In watts per $\mathrm{cm}^{2}$.

(b) In photons per $\mathrm{cm}^{2}$ per second (in the visible range). 

\section{Solution:}

(a) Assume that the sunlight falling on the moon is totally reflected by the moon's surface and that the intensity of the sunlight on the moon is the same as that on earth, which is $I$, the solar constant,

$$
I=1.4 \mathrm{~kW} / \mathrm{m}^{2} \text {. }
$$

Let $r$ be the radius of the moon, $R$ the distance between the moon and the earth. The flux of sunlight reflected from the moon per unit solid angle is $2 \pi r^{2} I / 2 \pi$. A unit area of the earth's surface subtends a solid angle $1 / R^{2}$ at the moon. Hence the intensity of moonlight on earth is

$$
I_{\mathrm{m}}=I(r / R)^{2} \approx 3 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{~W} / \mathrm{cm}^{2},
$$

taking $r=1.74 \times 10^{3} \mathrm{~km}, R=3.8 \times 10^{5} \mathrm{~km}$.

(b) The energy of a photon is $h \nu=h c / \lambda$. For the visible light, $\lambda \approx$ $5000 \AA$, and the number of photons arriving per $\mathrm{cm}^{2}$ per second is

$$
I_{\mathrm{m}} /(h c / \lambda)=7.5 \times 10^{12} \mathrm{~cm}^{-2} \mathrm{~s}^{-1} \text {. }
$$

\section{7}

Estimate the efficiency (visible watt/input watt) of 2 of the following light sources: gas light, incandescent bulb, light emitting diode, fluorescent tube, morcury of sodium arc, laser.

(Columbia)

\section{Solution:}

The luminous efficiency of a light source is given by the ratio of the amount of energy radiated per second in the wavelength range $4000-$ $7600 \AA$, which is commonly accepted as the visible range, to the total quantity of energy consumed by the source per second. Besides energy loss by conduction and convection, only a small fraction (the radiant efficiency) of the radiated energy is in the visible range. The luminous efficiencies of some common light sources are as follows:

gas light $0.001$ incandescent bulb $0.02$ fluorescent tube $0.09$ mercury arc $0.08$

sodium arc $0.08$

laser 0.003-0.4

Choose proper values from the given values below:

bulb?

$$

\section{9}

Give a description, including the physical principles involved, of the following optical systems:

(a) A lens or mirror arrangement for converting a $10 \mathrm{~cm}$ diameter spherical 1000 candle-power light-source into a $10^{6}$ beam candle-power search light. (Neglect lens or mirror light absorption.)

(b) An instrument capable of producing circularly-polarized light and of analysing light into its circularly-polarized components. (You may assume that the light is monochromatic).

(c) A system (in either the visible or radio-frequency range) for estimating the size of a distant (stellar) source of radiation.

(Columbia) 

\section{Solution:}

(a) A lens or concave mirror would converge light from a source placed near the focal point into a parallel beam as shown in Fig. 3.22.

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-201.jpg?height=382&width=1012&top_left_y=364&top_left_x=210)

Fig. $3.22$

Take the luminous intensity per unit solid angle of the source as $I(=$ 1000 candle-powers). Place the source near a lens or a concave mirror, which converges light within solid angle $\omega_{0}$ into a beam of solid angle $\omega^{\prime}$. The beam candle-power of the search light is

giving

$$
I \frac{\omega_{0}}{\omega^{\prime}}=I\left(\frac{\theta_{0}}{\theta^{\prime}}\right)^{2}=10^{3}\left(\frac{\theta_{0}}{\theta^{\prime}}\right)^{2}=10^{6},
$$

$$
\frac{\theta_{0}}{\theta^{\prime}}=31.6 \text {. }
$$

$\theta^{\prime} / \theta_{0}$ is the angular magnification. By geometry

and we have

$$
\theta^{\prime} f=\theta_{0} x,
$$

$$
x=\frac{f}{31.6} \text {. }
$$

$x$ is the required distance between the source and the focal point of the lens. Placing the source either to the left or the right of the focal point would produce a $10^{6}$ beam candle-power search light. (b)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-202.jpg?height=467&width=937&top_left_y=290&top_left_x=329)

Fig. $3.23$

Fig. $3.23$ shows an arrangement consisting of a linear polarizer and a quarter-wave plate placed in the beam sequentially, with their optic axes oriented at an angle of $45^{\circ}$. When a beam of unpolarized light passes through the polarizer, it becomes linearly polarized. As its electric field vector makes an angle of $45^{\circ}$ with the optic axis of the quareter-wave plate, the light emerging from the latter is circularly polarized. Beyond this, the arrangement consists of a $\frac{\lambda}{4}$-plate and a linear polarizer for detecting circularly polarized light. After passing through the $\lambda / 4$-plate, the circularly polarized light becomes linearly polarized. Rotating the linear polarizer that comes after it, the emerging intensity changes from maximum to sero showing the linear polarization of the light.

(c)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-202.jpg?height=401&width=957&top_left_y=1332&top_left_x=311)

Fig. $3.24$

In Michelson's stellar interferometer (Fig. 3.24), two plane mirrors $M_{1}$ and $M_{2}$ reflect light from a distant star onto two inclined mirrors $M_{3}$ and $M_{4}$ respectively. These in turn reflect the two parallel beams of light into a telescope objective after passing through the small slits $S_{1}$ and $S_{2}$ of separation d. Each beam forms an image of the star in the focal plane, and fringes are seen crossing the diffraction disc of the combined image. The visibility of the fringes depends on $h$. As $h$ is increased, the first disappearence of fringes occurs at

$$
h=1.22 \frac{\lambda}{\alpha},
$$

giving the angular sise $\alpha$ of the star disk.

\section{0}

\section{Metallic diffraction grating.}

Purcell (Phys. Rev. 92, 1069) has shown that electromagnetic radiation is emitted from a metallic diffraction grating when a beam of charged particles passes near and parallel to the surface of the grating.

The velocity of the charged particles is $v$ and the grating spacing is $d$. Consider only the light emitted in the plane perpendicular to the grating and containing the particle trajectory. tion?

(a) How does the frequency of the emitted radiation depend on direc-

(b) Suppose the metallic grating is replaced by a transmission grating, e.g., a piece of glass with grooves ruled onto it, still $d$ apart. In what way, if any, will this change the emitted radiation? Explain qualitatively.

(Wisconsin)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-203.jpg?height=394&width=600&top_left_y=1402&top_left_x=112)

(a)

![](https://cdn.mathpix.com/cropped/2022_11_07_118210d0a2720794b185g-203.jpg?height=329&width=486&top_left_y=1416&top_left_x=769)

(b)

Fis. $3.25$ 

\section{Solution:}

(a) The passing charged particles induce the electrons inside the metallic grating to move. The motion is limited by the very fine wires of the grating and the electrons are decelerated, which causes bremsstrahlung. Consider radiations emitted in XOZ plane (see Fig. 3.25) making an sngle $\theta$ with the normal to the gratings. The radiations emitted from any two adjacent wires would have a delay in time of

$$
\Delta t=\frac{d}{v}-\frac{d \cos \theta}{c} .
$$

When $c \Delta t=m \lambda$, where $m$ is an integer, the radiations interfere constructively. Thus the frequency observed at $\theta$ is given by

$$
\lambda(\theta)=\frac{d}{m}\left(\frac{c}{v}-\cos \theta\right) .
$$

For $m=1, \lambda(\theta)=d\left(\frac{\varepsilon}{v}-\cos \theta\right)$.

(b) As electrons in glass are not free to move, no bremsstrahlung takes place, i.e., no radiation is emitted.
